He typed `500` and stopped. Five hundred covered almost none of what she had listed. It would look like insult money, payment as theater. He erased it and typed `2000`, added `tuition deferment` in the memo field, and hit submit.

The screen froze for three seconds, then dropped a new line:

`Transfer paused: new-device verification required. Code sent to phone ending 7734.`

7734 was the smartphone number he had killed on day two, cracked screen and dead battery in a gas-station trash can outside Wheeling. He tapped the support link anyway. After six minutes of menu prompts and hold music, a live rep told him they could release the transfer if he took a security callback on the primary number or logged in from a previously recognized device. He said, "I don't have either," and sounded like a man reporting weather instead of choices. He hung up before she could offer branch options.

A mosquito landed on his wrist and fed while he read Nora's last message again. He did not swat it until it flew off swollen and slow. A perfect little transaction.

By one-thirty he was back in the motel lot with the hood up, not because he planned to fix anything but because standing there with tools in his hand looked like action. He tightened the battery terminals with a socket wrench he barely needed. He wiped the dipstick and checked oil that had not changed color since yesterday. He rewrapped electrical tape around a cracked coolant hose. He knew exactly what he was doing: building a case file against immediate transfer, piece by piece, like evidence for a jury he had invented.

At one-fifty-two, eight minutes before her deadline, he sent a text.

`Tried sending 2000. Transfer got locked to dead-number verification (7734). I'm on support now.`

He read it back: process, caveat, process. Still no receipt.

No reply came.

At two-oh-seven she sent one line.

`Then clear verification and send the confirmation number.`

At two-eleven, another message arrived.

`Attorney appointment confirmed for 3:30 PM. If transfer posts before then, I pause filing tonight.`

At two-fifteen:

`For planning: if no transfer posts by tonight, I move ahead with account protections tomorrow at 9:00 AM.`

He laughed once, a short sound with no humor, because even her threat came formatted as scheduling. He started a reply, then locked the screen and set the phone face down on the passenger seat. A fly walked circles on the dashboard vent.

The sun shifted west and turned the windshield into a white plate. His shirt stuck to the seatback. He opened the folder again and spread the documents across the steering wheel: tuition portal printout, mortgage coupon, his handwritten budget, the Mather estimate, an old family insurance card with all four names on it. The paper edges curled in the heat.

A new email hit the burner inbox while he stared at the stack. Nora had forwarded a bursar screenshot with no note attached.

`TEMPORARY DEFERMENT ACTIVE THROUGH FRIDAY 12:00 PM ET.`  
`Initial payment received: $600.00.`  
`Guarantor on file: Nora Mercer.`  
`Remaining balance due: $5,880.00.`

Under those lines, in smaller text, the portal showed: `deferment processing fee applied.`

He copied the damage onto his legal pad instead of running probabilities:

Road checking untouched: $12,984.22  
Nora's grocery card charged today: $600.00  
June's access expires Friday noon without the remaining payment: $5,880.00 plus fee

When he closed his eyes he saw June at move-in carrying the blue plastic fan she insisted on buying at the campus store because "the dorm AC is fake." She had balanced two bins at once and rolled her shoulders back with that runner posture, impatient with every parent who moved too slowly in the stairwell. He had felt proud and useless at the same time, a split feeling he had never learned how to hold without running from one side into the other. He opened his eyes and the fan became the motel desk lamp cord coiled in front of him like a dead snake.

At three-twenty-five his phone buzzed with an incoming call from Nora. He answered before the first ring finished.

"I'm walking in now," she said. Street noise, then a door, then muffled office quiet. "You've got one minute before I sit down."

He straightened in the seat though she could not see him. "I can move something if I call the bank line again—"

"Did you clear the verification lock?"

"No, not yet. If I stay on them and force a manual release, I can still move something today, but it won't cover all of it."

"Eli. Did you clear it."

He looked at the bank app. He had already logged in. The cursor blinked in the amount field, waiting.

"No. I didn't. I hung up."

"Then this isn't a bank problem. It's you."

He closed the app without submitting anything. It took effort, like setting down something heavy with one hand.

"I'm not pretending otherwise. I can't take the full hit today," he said. "I'm choosing the road over you and June, and yeah, my hands are shaking while I say it."

She drew one small inhale, controlled, almost professional. "Then we're done for now."

"Nora, wait—just thirty seconds."

"I'm not arguing with absence and spreadsheets at the same time. Send actual money with a confirmation number, or don't."

"I can maybe do fifteen hundred by—"

"Maybe is not a number."

"Two thousand if the rep picks up and releases the lock."

"That is still a condition."

He heard a receptionist in the background say, "Ms. Mercer?" and Nora answer, "Yes, one second." Then she returned to him for one final sentence, even and precise.

"I'm documenting this call as no transfer and no amount committed."

The line cut.

He sat without moving until the car's heat made his calves cramp. His right hand still jittered against the phone hinge, so he pinned it under his thigh. He was still choosing control over repair, and he could feel the choice in the cramp climbing his calf. In his head the transfer screen kept blinking the same blank amount field.

At three-fifty she replied.

`You do not get to disappear and then call this confusion. Return funds today.`

Eight minutes later June texted for the first time that day.

`Bursar gave me a temporary card through Friday noon.`

`Mom had to sign for it in front of me.`

`Did you take money from emergency?`

His throat closed on a sour swallow. He got out of the car, braced both hands on the hot hood, and dry-heaved twice into gravel that smelled like oil and old beer. Nothing came up except spit and bitter coffee. He typed `I'll explain tonight.` and erased it. He left June's question unanswered.

By five he had checked out of the motel and loaded the car: folder under the passenger seat, duffel in the trunk, water bottles wedged behind the driver's seat so they would not roll. The clerk with the sunflower tattoo handed him a receipt and said, "You forgot your pen," holding up the motel-branded ballpoint he had used for his numbers. He took it and thanked her. On her right wrist she wore three rubber bands snapped together into a bracelet, one pink, one green, one black. She tugged it once as she watched him walk out, a nervous little ritual he recognized because he had his own, thumb to phone hinge, over and over.

At the interstate on-ramp, traffic thickened into evening trucks. Tires hissed on soft asphalt. A billboard for a personal injury firm promised `WE FIGHT FOR YOU` over a photo of a smiling man in a tie. Eli merged west and kept the speed at sixty-two to baby the radiator, both hands on the wheel, shoulders tight enough that his neck burned.

He told himself he had bought time, maybe forty-eight hours and maybe less. At sixty-two, with the fan belt whining and the coolant needle one notch under hot, he said the numbers out loud like a prayer: six hundred on Nora's grocery card, Friday noon deferment edge, nine a.m. legal filing. Each repetition tightened his jaw until his back teeth hurt.

Under that running chant sat the harder line he could not smooth: Nora had sent proof. Nora had sent deadlines. June had now seen the deferment with Nora's signature and asked him directly if he had taken the money. He had chosen delay in full view of both of them.

By the time the sky went copper over the cornfields, his phone had gone quiet. There were no new texts, no unknown numbers yet from lawyers or servicers. There was only road noise and engine whine and the faint sweet smell of coolant every time the wind shifted through the vents.

At a rest area outside Effingham he parked under a dead light pole and ate cold crackers for dinner. A family at the next table argued gently over a map app that would not rotate. A little boy in a Pirates cap kept dropping a plastic dinosaur and wiping it clean on his shirt before setting it on the table again, careful, ceremonial. Eli watched the boy do it three times and thought of June at eight lining up her pencils by color before school, then shoving the whole row into her backpack at the last second because she did not want anyone to see she cared that much.

In the dark, the rest area vending machine buzzed and clicked and dropped someone's chips with a metal clang that echoed in the concrete shelter. Eli leaned back on the bench and felt the wood slats through his shirt, each one pressing a separate ridge into his spine. He opened the transfer page again. The signal was strong. The account was open. No lock stopped him now.

He was not waiting on the bank anymore; he was preserving control of the road and spending June's deadline to do it. His thumb cramped white at the knuckle above the `Amount` field, and his jaw ticked with each breath. He watched his thumb hover over the keypad and drift away.

No new texts came after June's question.

He put the phone in the glove compartment, closed it, and rested both palms on the steering wheel as if he were already moving. In front of him the interstate pulled west in two bright lanes, trucks stitching red lights into the dark. Behind him, in Pittsburgh, his wife had converted hurt into procedure and his daughter's tuition had become a clock. The money stayed where he had put it. The engine cooled with small ticking sounds under the hood, and when he finally turned the key the fan belt squealed once before catching. He pulled back onto the interstate with both windows cracked and kept it under sixty as he aimed west for St. Louis.


# Chapter 5

Hours later, South St. Louis came on in low brick and sodium light as Eli crossed the river. The patched radiator had started its thin kettle whine again, a high note under the engine that he could feel through the steering wheel. The sedan smelled like old coffee and the sweet chemical edge of coolant. He drove with both windows cracked because he did not trust the gauge and because the air inside the car had turned stale with his own shirt and fear.

Theo's block sat under a row of flat-roof brick buildings with narrow porches and dented mailboxes bolted crooked at shoulder height. Somebody had parked a rusting grill half on the sidewalk, half in the dirt strip where grass used to be. A television thumped through an open downstairs window in quick bursts of crowd noise and ad jingles. Eli rolled past once, then circled again because he had missed the number and because he had still not decided what words he was going to use.

He parked in front of a fire hydrant anyway. He told himself it was two minutes. He shut the engine off and sat with his hands on the wheel while the metal clicked and cooled. Nora's message thread was open on his burner. The last text from her had come at three in the afternoon.

`You do not get to disappear and then call this confusion. Return funds today.`

He could have answered. He had answered versions of that message in his head for two days, each version sounding calm and practical until he looked at the numbers in his green folder and watched calm collapse into arithmetic. Mortgage arrears. June's tuition bill. The transfer he had made from emergency savings before he left, fourteen thousand dollars moved in twelve taps while Nora was on an overnight ICU shift and June was packing dish soap and twin XL sheets.

He locked the car, took the folder, and left his duffel in the trunk. If Theo shut the door in his face, Eli did not want to stand on the sidewalk holding everything he had like a man evicted before he even asked.

The stairwell smelled like hot dust and someone else's fried fish. Paint peeled from the handrail in curled strips that lifted against his palm. By the second landing his right knee had started its familiar complaint, the old stiffness from years of pretending office chairs and conference rooms did not count as injury. He paused at apartment 2B and listened. He heard a sink running, then a cabinet closing hard.

He knocked once. He waited. He knocked again, louder.

The door opened to the chain stop. Theo looked through the gap first, one eye and half his face, then unlatched the chain and pulled it wider with his bare foot braced against the jamb.

Theo had gotten wider across the chest since Eli had seen him last spring at Nora's birthday dinner. The sleeve tattoos climbed both forearms in dense black and muted green, florals tangled with block lettering Eli had never asked about. His hair was clipped short on the sides. He wore a faded Local 304 shirt and gray work pants with something dark ground into one knee. A square of nicotine gum bulged his cheek while he looked Eli over from shoes to hairline like he was checking a pallet before accepting delivery.

"You lost?" Theo said.

Eli tried to smile and felt it miss his face. "No. I came to see you."

"At nine-thirty on a Tuesday with no call?" Theo glanced past him to the stairwell. "Where's your car?"

"Out front."

"You drove here from Pittsburgh."

"I drove here from Indiana." Eli shifted the folder from one hand to the other. "Can I come in for a minute?"

Theo kept his hand on the doorknob. He did not move. "Mom said you vanished four days ago. She also said you took fourteen grand before you bailed."

Eli looked down at the threshold strip, the brass worn dull in the center where shoes hit. "I know what she told you."

"Yeah?"

"Most of it's true." He forced himself to look back up. "I need a place to sleep for two nights. That's what I'm asking."

Theo did not answer. The sink in the apartment shut off. A woman called from deeper inside, "T, who is it?"

Theo said, without taking his eyes off Eli, "It's my dad."

The pause from inside stretched long enough for Eli to hear the refrigerator compressor kick on. Then the woman said, "Do you want me out here?"

"Yeah."

Theo opened the door another two inches but still blocked most of it with his body. "You don't get to walk in and do a reunion scene. You hear me?"

"I'm not here for that."

"Good." Theo stepped back one pace. "Then come in and tell the truth fast."

The apartment was narrow and clean in the way clean gets when two people have jobs and no spare time. Shoes lined one wall in matched pairs under a shelf of books and folded grocery totes. A fan clicked in the living room window. The couch was low and gray, one arm patched with black thread in tidy stitches. A wooden crate beside it held a stack of library books, three coasters, and a mason jar full of loose change arranged by coin type. Quarters in one row, dimes in another.

Imani stood at the edge of the kitchen with a dish towel in one hand. Her sidecut was grown out a little, tight curls clipped back with a copper barrette. She wore wire-frame glasses that kept sliding down her nose, and every time they dropped she pushed them up with the back of her wrist so the dishwater would not smear the lenses. A legal pad lay open on the counter under a mug. Eli could see three highlighted lines and a sticky note that said `fellowship app - submit Fri 11:59 PM` in block capitals.

"Hi, Mr. Mercer," she said. Her tone was polite and level, but she did not smile. "Do you need water?"

He took the glass in both hands, more grateful than he wanted to show. "Thanks. Eli is fine."

She filled a glass from the tap and set it on the table. Theo stayed standing while Eli sat, which made Eli feel like he had asked for a bank loan and was waiting for terms.

Theo said, "Start with where you've been. Then tell us why you came here before going home."

Eli drank half the water too fast and put the glass down carefully. "I dropped June off. I started back. I couldn't do it. I kept driving. I had a radiator blow on the west side of Indianapolis. I worked a kitchen shift for cash to cover part of the repair."

"You worked a kitchen shift," Theo repeated.

"At a diner by the tow yard."

"Okay. Keep going."

Eli pressed his thumb into the wet ring under the glass. "I told myself another hundred miles would buy me one clean sentence before I called anybody. Instead I stacked miles and excuses. That was dumb. I know it."

Theo gave one short laugh that had no humor in it. "You thought disappearing was strategy."

"I thought I had twenty-four hours. Then it turned into more." His fingers worried the damp edge of the glass. "I did buy a burner. I didn't want location apps on me."

"You didn't want accountability on you," Theo said.

"That too." Eli rubbed his forehead and stopped when he felt sweat and road grit come away on his fingers. "I took money before I left. I planned to put it back quick."

Theo's jaw worked. He spit the gum into a napkin, folded it, and set it down with precise little presses of his thumb as if neatness could keep him from yelling.

"How much do you have right now?" Theo asked.

"On me?"

"Total."

"Cash and bank, about twelve-six." Eli corrected himself. "Twelve thousand six hundred."

Imani spoke for the first time since the water. "The transfer was fourteen."

"I know." Eli ran a thumb along the legal pad's wire spiral until it bit his skin. "I covered repairs and gas and motel nights." He pulled the green folder out and put it on the table. "I've got receipts."

Theo leaned one hand on the chair back across from Eli and looked down at the folder but did not touch it. "You brought proof before you brought an apology."

"I brought both."

"I don't hear one yet."

Eli kept his hands flat on the table. He had rehearsed this part in parking lots and truck-stop bathrooms and while staring at motel ceilings that hummed with cheap fluorescent light. Every rehearsal had him sounding composed. Sitting in Theo's kitchen, he heard the manager voice come out anyway, the one that lined facts in neat rows to keep panic from showing.

"I'm sorry I left without telling anybody. I'm sorry I moved the money. I'm sorry I put Mom in this position with tuition and the mortgage due." He stopped and swallowed. "I'm asking for two nights. I need to sleep somewhere and figure out how to put the cash back by Friday."

Theo crossed his arms. "Don't use her deadlines to make this sound noble."

"I'm not."

"You did this."

"I know I did."

"Do you?"

The question grabbed him at the throat. Heat climbed his neck, and the water he had swallowed a minute ago sat heavy and sour in his gut. He looked at his son, at the scar under Theo's chin from a bike crash at ten, at the fresh burn mark on Theo's forearm that looked like a forklift battery incident, at the five-year sobriety medallion hanging from a nail by the fridge with his keys. Theo noticed where he was looking and stepped so his shoulder blocked the medallion from view.

"Don't do that," Theo said.

"Do what?"

"Don't look at that chip like it proves you're part of it."

Imani lifted the dish towel and folded it over once. "Theo."

"No, let him hear it." Theo looked back at Eli. "You want to know what this feels like? It feels familiar."

Eli's neck tightened against his collar. "Theo."

"No. You're going to listen now." Theo pulled out the chair and sat down hard, knees wide, hands flat. "You keep saying this week got away from you. Like weather. Like traffic. Like a calendar accident. But I've got a date for this exact move." He pointed at Eli with two fingers. "Saturday, July seventeenth, 2021. Family session at St. Bart's Recovery, South County, ten a.m."

Eli stared at him. The room narrowed, fan click and refrigerator hum growing louder while everything else dropped out.

"I was twenty-two," Theo said. "Three weeks sober and still shaking in group because my body hadn't decided what to do without vodka. Counselor says family session, bring one parent minimum. Mom had a double at Barnes and couldn't get off. You said you'd come. You texted at nine-forty that you were parking."

Eli remembered typing in the hospital garage with his hazard lights blinking, remembered deciding he had fifteen minutes to fix one compliance report before he walked into the room where his son would ask him for things Eli did not know how to give. He remembered opening the report, then another email, then another.

Theo kept going, voice flat now, almost gentle in the way hard facts can be when anger gets tired of carrying itself. "At ten-thirty, counselor asks if we should start without you. I said no, he's coming. At eleven, she asks again. I said traffic's bad. At eleven-thirty I went to the vending machine and called you from the hallway because my phone got locked in meds storage. You answered. I heard your office printer in the background."

Imani looked down at the table. Eli did not move.

"You said, and I remember this word for word, that you'd had a work emergency and we'd do a longer talk tomorrow. Tomorrow never happened. You sent me forty bucks on Venmo with a thumbs-up emoji at seven that night." Theo laughed again, short and bitter. "That was your fatherhood for the month. Forty dollars and a thumb."

Eli breathed in through his nose and smelled dish soap, hot onion from somebody else's dinner downstairs, and the metallic tang of his own sweat dried into his shirt. He felt every inch of the chair under him, the press of cheap plywood through his jeans, the wobble in one back leg.

"I remember the Venmo," he said.

"You don't get points for remembering the worst part."

"I remember all of it." Eli's voice roughened on the last word. "I parked across from St. Bart's at nine-fifty-two and watched the automatic doors open and close. I told myself I'd walk in after one email, then after one more. At ten-fifteen I was still in the car chewing the skin off my thumb and watching people carry coffee into the lobby."

Theo's eyes narrowed, suspicious of any sentence that sounded too tidy.

Eli kept going before he could retreat into explanation. "I heard you on voicemail from the hallway at eleven-thirty. I knew that cinder-block echo. I said work emergency because I couldn't stand up and let you tell that room what I'd done to you. I called you lazy when you were shaking. I called you dramatic when you were detoxing. And when you needed me in one plastic chair, I hid in a parking lot and sent forty bucks like a tip. I started to tell myself I froze, but that's still a dodge. I didn't miss that session. I bailed."

Silence settled across the table. The fan clicked, clicked, clicked.

Imani set the dish towel down and pulled the legal pad closer. "Then we're not doing comfort tonight. We're doing terms."

Theo tipped his chin toward Imani, then tapped the folder with two fingers. "How many drinks have you had today?"

"None."

"Yesterday?"

"None."

Theo waited.

Eli felt the pause cinch around his throat. If he lied here, he was done before midnight.
"I had two beers Sunday night in Indiana," Eli said. "I haven't had anything since."

Theo dragged his thumb along the table edge, jaw tight. "I'm not running a breathalyzer checkpoint in my own living room. But I'm not doing surprises either."

"Yeah, I hear you." It still came out in Eli's old policy voice, polished and bloodless. "You won't get surprises from me."

Imani pulled the legal pad closer, flipped to a clean page, and wrote the date in the top right corner. Her handwriting was small and square, each letter separate as if she had trained herself against ambiguity. She clicked her pen twice.

"If you stay," she said, "it's two nights. Tonight and tomorrow. You leave by eight a.m. Thursday unless we all agree on something else."

Eli looked from her to Theo. Theo did not object.

Forty-eight hours was enough time to wire money or prove Theo right again.
"Okay," Eli said.

"Rule two," Imani said. "No alcohol in this apartment. No showing up smelling like a bar. No hidden bottles in the car."

"Yeah. I won't bring any in."

"Rule three. Quiet hours after ten-thirty because Theo clocks in at five and I've got class at eight. If you come in late, you text first and keep it silent."

He pictured himself fumbling with the lock after midnight and Theo listening from behind a shut bedroom door.
"I can do that."

"Rule four. Chores are not symbolic." She tapped the pen against the pad. "You do dinner dishes tonight and tomorrow. You take trash and recycling down in the morning before seven-thirty. If laundry is in the basket by the bathroom, you fold it instead of stepping over it."

Humiliation flashed hot in his face and burned off. He had scrubbed diner pans for cash this week; this was not the line that mattered.
Eli blinked. "Laundry."

Imani met his eyes. "Yes. Laundry."

He scratched his thumbnail against the table seam, then stilled his hand. "Okay."

Theo leaned back and watched Eli with that same intake expression, the way he used to inspect used tires when he was sixteen and spending weekends at the shop two blocks from Nora's cousin's place. "Rule five," Theo said. "No father speeches. No fixing me. No postmortem on my life. You answer direct questions direct. You don't perform remorse to get comfort."

The line made Eli's shoulders jerk. For a second he reached for the old shield, father as job title, and felt it fail in his chest.
"I won't."

"And you don't talk to Mom from here unless it's money logistics. If you want to do tears and history, take that outside." Theo looked toward the bedroom door. "I'm not making her hear you beg."

His chest tightened anyway at the image of Nora hearing him through these thin walls.
"Yeah."

"Say it like a sentence," Theo said.

"It's fair. I won't put that on her."

Imani wrote while they talked, then turned the pad around so both men could see. She had listed each rule with checkboxes. At the bottom she had written, `If any rule breaks, stay ends immediately.`

Eli stared at the page and felt an old reflex rise in him, hot and stupid, the reflex that said parent means authority, father means exception, this is my son and I don't sign terms in his kitchen. The reflex that had ruined him in smaller installments for years before the interstate made the damage obvious.

Theo's voice carried no malice, only a practiced caution he had paid dearly to learn.

"Do you want me to sign it?" Eli asked.

Imani said, "Yes."

He took the pen. His hand trembled once on the downstroke of the E. He signed and passed it to Theo.

Theo hesitated, then signed under him.

"I'm not doing this to humiliate you," Theo said.

Eli set the pen down. "I know."

"I'm doing it because the old version of you shows up when things get uncomfortable, and he breaks stuff."

"I know that too."

Imani folded the page and tucked it under the sugar canister by the stove. She checked the time on her phone, then nudged it toward Theo with her knuckles. "What does Nora need first?"

"Transfer clock," Theo said. He turned to Eli. "By three p.m. tomorrow, you send at least five grand and you send both of us the confirmation. If it doesn't clear, you pack before dinner."

Eli set both palms flat on the table. "It'll clear."

"Don't sell confidence," Theo said. "Say what you'll do."

"I'll send five by three. Screenshot to both of you."

"Good." Imani capped her pen. "Now shower, then eat, then text Nora the plan before you sleep. No surprises."

In the bathroom he turned the water on and watched it run brown for two seconds before going clear. He peeled his shirt off and saw his own body in the mirror under yellow light: broad still, but softened at the waist; shoulders slumped; road grit at his collarbone; a bruise on his hip from crawling under the car in Indiana to tighten the radiator clamp. Through the vent he heard Theo and Imani talking low over the rattle of a pan.

"You okay?" Imani asked.

"Yeah."

"If he misses the transfer window?"

"He's out tomorrow night."

Eli stood under the spray until the hot water thinned, then dried off and put on one of Theo's spare T-shirts, black with a cracked print from a closed punk venue on Cherokee Street. Back in the kitchen, Theo slid scrambled eggs and toast toward him and stayed standing.

"What happens Thursday morning?" Theo asked.

Eli opened the green folder. "If I keep enough gas money, I can send five by three tomorrow and maybe three more by Friday. After that I'd have to pick up day labor. Kansas City, maybe Denver."

"That's drifting with a spreadsheet." Theo pointed at the page. "No fantasy route talk. Tomorrow's transfer, then a real next move."

Denver came out of Eli's mouth like a highway sign, bright and empty. He felt the old relief of talking about routes instead of damage, and he hated how fast that relief arrived.

"I don't have a real next move yet."

"Then say that."

"I don't have one yet."

Imani was already at her laptop, tabs and notes spread around her like a second workspace. "Text Nora numbers only," she said without looking up. "Don't send her a confession memo at midnight."

"I won't."

"And don't open my office door," she said. "One spilled cup in there costs me six months."

Eli lifted his hands an inch, surrender plain in the gesture. "I won't touch it."

The office door sat ten feet away, white paint chipped at the latch. He had no intention of crossing that line, but the warning still scraped him raw; in this apartment every threshold had terms.

He did the dishes while Theo set out a thin blanket and one flat pillow. Theo checked his alarms, then paused in the doorway to the bedroom.

"You came to my graduation and said parking was impossible," Theo said. "Nora told me last month you had a panic attack in the lot and left."

Eli shut off the faucet.

Heat jumped behind Eli's ears. He remembered sitting in the lot with his suit jacket folded on the passenger seat while families streamed past in black gowns and camera flashes. He had counted breaths until the ceremony started without him.

"I can work with ugly facts," Theo said. "I can't work with cleaned-up ones."

"I hear you."

"Lock's automatic. Don't deadbolt unless you're sure I'm in."

The bedroom door clicked shut.

Eli pulled the couch bed out, got it stuck, remembered lift left then drag, and heard the frame drop into place with a metal clank that sounded too loud for the room. He opened Nora's thread and typed, deleted, typed again.

`I'm at Theo's in St. Louis. I'll transfer 5000 by 3 PM tomorrow and send confirmation when it clears.`

He sent it and set the phone face down. Sleep came in short strips between other people's noises: a toilet upstairs, a motorcycle one block over, pipes ticking in the wall. At four-forty he woke to coffee and saw Theo in the hall pulling on steel-toe boots.

"You up?" Theo whispered.

"Yeah."

"Trash goes down before seven-thirty. Bags are by the back door. Meeting tonight at six. If you take this second night, you're in that room for the full hour."

Eli pushed up on one elbow. "Okay."

"Don't do okay voice. Do it or don't."

Eli held his gaze. "I'll go."

Theo rapped two knuckles against the doorframe and left.

At six-thirty Imani came out in leggings and an oversized museum sweatshirt, hair damp from a rushed shower. She weighed coffee grounds on a digital scale and scraped two grams back into the jar.

"Did you sleep?" she asked.

"Enough."

She glanced at the tied trash bags by the door. "Thank you for not needing a second reminder."

"Low bar."

"Bars exist." She poured water into the kettle. "Go to the meeting tonight. Your pattern is motion. Sit still with witnesses for one hour."

"Theo said the same thing."

"Then listen to him for once." She zipped her bag, then stopped with her hand on the strap. "If Nora calls me, I'm not covering for you."

"You shouldn't."

"Good. Then we're clear."

After she left, the apartment felt larger and meaner. Eli stripped the couch, stacked blanket and pillow where Theo had left them, then spread his paperwork across the table. Mortgage notice. Tuition statement. Indiana repair invoice. ATM receipt that now read like theft. He wrote three transfer scenarios and crossed out two.

At 8:53 his phone buzzed.

Nora: `Funds by 3 PM today. Confirm exact amount.`

He typed and erased twice before sending:

`5000 by 3 PM. Screenshot right after.`

Nora: `Do it.`

At 2:47 he opened the banking app with both hands because his thumb had started to shake. His knee bounced under the table hard enough to rattle the sugar canister. He entered `5000.00`, backed out, entered it again, then pressed `CONFIRM` and listened to the fan click like a metronome in his teeth.

The spinner froze on the third turn. For a second he thought the transfer had failed and felt panic flash hot in his mouth, metallic as a bitten lip. At fourteen seconds the confirmation banner appeared. He exhaled once, hard, and sent the screenshot to Nora and Theo before he could talk himself into checking it again.

Nora: `I see it. Thank you for moving fast. This covers today, not what you did. Friday by noon for the next transfer.`

Theo: `Good. Meeting still 6.`

At four-thirty Theo came home with forklift dust on his boots and a sweat-darkened collar. He checked the sugar canister where the rules sat folded, then looked at Eli.

"Money hit," Theo said. "One condition left. You walk into that meeting with me and you stay till closing prayer. You bail, second night's gone."

"I'm going."

"Shoes by the door. Ten minutes."

Eli sat long enough to feel his pulse in his gums, then laced his boots and followed Theo down the stairs.

The meeting met in the basement of a brick church three blocks over, in a room that smelled like burnt coffee and mop water. Metal folding chairs made a rough circle under fluorescent tubes that buzzed loud enough to blur the edges of conversation. A hand-lettered sign on the wall read, `Take what you need and leave the rest.`

At the doorway Theo caught Eli's sleeve. "No observer mode. If you talk, you talk plain."

"Plain," Eli said.

Ray, a gray-bearded man with an enamel name tag clipped to his sweatshirt, looked up from the coffee urn. "Theo, you bringing someone in?"

"My dad," Theo said. He did not lower his voice. "He's here because he runs when things get hot."

A few heads turned. Sweat gathered between Eli's shoulder blades in the cold room.

Ray held out a paper cup. "Then he's in the right room. Grab a chair, both of you."

They sat side by side. When check-in reached newcomers, Eli heard his pulse before he heard his voice.

"I'm Eli. I emptied our emergency account and disappeared for four days. My son told me if I wanted his couch, I had to sit in this chair and stop lying."

Theo leaned toward him, voice low and flat. "Say the amount."

Eli swallowed. "Fourteen thousand dollars."

Theo sat back, hands unclenching on his knees.

By the time closing prayer came, Eli's knee had locked from the metal chair and his palms smelled like burnt coffee from gripping the paper cup too hard. He stood anyway, shoulder to shoulder with Theo while the circle closed.

Outside, the air had turned sharp. Theo walked half a block before speaking. "Second night stands. Don't make me regret saying that."

"I won't," Eli said, and this time it sounded like a promise instead of policy language.

Back in the apartment kitchen, Theo pulled the rule sheet from under the sugar canister, checked the box beside `meeting`, and slid it back into place.
The second night held.


# Chapter 6

Quick taps from Theo's short chef's knife reached Eli in the hallway, onions piling past the edge of a cutting board too small for the job. It was the night after closing prayer beside Theo, and he followed the sound into the kitchen.

Eli stood at the stove with a wooden spoon in one hand and a bag of frozen peas sweating onto his wrist. The apartment had the late-evening smell of cumin and laundry detergent and that burnt-dust radiator smell that showed up the first cool nights of fall, even before the building turned the heat on for real.

Imani came in from the bedroom tying her sidecut back with a black satin scarf, wire-frame glasses low on her nose, one sock still in her hand. She hopped twice toward the kitchen island, slid her foot into the sock without touching the floor, and said, "Somebody tell me if we're doing rice in a pot or rice in that cursed little rice cooker before I commit to either timeline."

"Pot," Theo said. "Cursed little rice cooker makes glue."

"That thing has one mood," Imani said. "And that mood is kindergarten paste."

Eli turned down the burner. "I can run to the store if you're short on anything."

Theo scraped onions into a bowl. "We're good. Sit down if your knee's barking."

"It's fine."

His knee was not fine. It had a weather report all its own and had been issuing warnings since they carried June's mini fridge up three dorm flights in August. He shifted his weight anyway, like he could bluff his body the way he used to bluff auditors.

Theo opened a spice tin, pinched in paprika, then handed Eli a raw chicken thigh without looking at him. "Salt both sides first. Don't bury it in the pan wet." He said it the same way he'd say, Buckle that pallet before it tips, all timing and consequence, no lecture.

Eli salted. He patted with paper towel. He listened for the first clean hiss when the chicken touched hot oil and felt a ridiculous pulse of relief at getting one small thing right.

Across from him, Imani lined up three chipped bowls in a row and dropped measured scoops of yogurt sauce into each one. She tasted a spoonful, frowned, and added lemon. Then she reached behind the toaster and pulled out a folded museum brochure, opened it flat, and started writing her Tuesday seminar notes on the blank margin in tiny block letters.

Eli said, "You still doing that master's program and the desk job?"

Imani looked up. "Yep. Two classes this term. I work admissions at the museum Wednesday through Saturday, ten to two. Then reading till my eyes mutiny."

"She's writing eighty pages on how rich people stole half of Europe," Theo said.

"I'm writing eighty pages on how institutions rename theft until donors can applaud it," she said.

Theo barked a laugh. "See? That's why she gets the A's."

Eli smiled before he could stop himself. The sound of Theo laughing was lower than it had been as a teenager, less sharp around the edges. In high school it had always come with a dare attached. Now it sounded like something earned and rationed.

Imani pointed at the pan. "Flip those before they turn into roof shingles."

He flipped them. The skin had gone amber and spotted in places where the heat had run hotter. It looked good enough.

On the little speaker by the sink, an old soul track came up with a bass line Eli knew but couldn't place. Theo named it in the first two notes. Imani shouted from the pantry, "No, wrong year, that's the remaster."

Theo held up a finger. "Original pressing, seventy-five."

"Try seventy-three, forklift boy."

They were both wrong. The singer announced the song on a live clip before the first verse, date and venue included, and Theo slapped his own forehead with a wet hand.

"I stand corrected by a dead man," he said.

Imani grinned. "That's your best kind of correction."

Eli laughed. It surprised all three of them, including him. The sound came out loud and unplanned, not the conference-call courtesy breath, and it hitched once in his chest. Theo glanced at him and shook his head.

"Careful," Theo said. "Next you'll be arguing liner notes."

"I can barely read the back of a cereal box without my glasses now," Eli said.

"Welcome to the club," Imani said. "My bag is basically glass cases and chargers."

She said it and reached into her tote and produced two pairs of folded readers, one in a cracked green case, one wrapped in a sock. Theo pointed at the sock pair.

"Those are your emergency emergency glasses?"

"Those are my emergency emergency emergency glasses," she said.

Theo put the back of his hand to his forehead like fainting. "She's unstoppable."

The timer on Eli's burner beeped. He turned off the heat, moved the chicken to a plate, and watched steam cloud the kitchen window over the sink. Outside was the parking lot and then the alley, both lit the same tired sodium orange as every lot he'd slept in for the last week. Inside, Theo had magnets on the refrigerator from places Eli had never heard him mention: a state park in Arkansas, a cat cafe in Chicago, an auto parts sponsor picnic in Alton. There were two dry-erase calendars side by side. One held Imani's class deadlines in blue marker, each reading assignment boxed and checked. The other held Theo's shift bids, overtime blocks, and Tuesday/Thursday marks that read MEETING 7:30 in neat print.

Eli had been in this apartment for thirty-six hours and only now clocked that the meetings were always at the same time, always in the same handwriting, no flourish. He had known Theo was sober. The dry-erase blocks on the fridge showed the labor of staying that way.

Warehouse work, he'd known. He hadn't pictured shift bids, union steward calls, and a text thread that could blow up over forklift tire policies. He'd clocked the tattoos too, but only now noticed one date in Roman numerals climbing from Theo's wrist, plus the initials of a man Eli had never met.

"Rice," Imani said.

He snapped back and moved to drain it. He almost poured the water into the sink without the colander. Theo caught his wrist light and quick.

"Not straight down," Theo said. "You'll make wallpaper paste in the pipes. Use the strainer."

"Right."

Imani kept her eyes on the sauce. "Apartment plumbing is like emotional plumbing. It'll take one weird decision and punish everybody."

Theo snorted. "That why the upstairs guy flooded his bathroom again?"

"That was not emotional," she said. "That was tequila plus bad grout."

Before he sat, he pulled the flip phone from his pocket and killed the vibration. Nine missed calls stacked under the lock-screen clock, all from hospital numbers, and one voicemail transcript from Cal still glowed at the top: FINAL REQUEST FOR IMMEDIATE CONTACT TODAY TO AVOID ABANDONMENT. He turned the phone face down by his water glass and told himself he'd open the inbox after dishes.

They ate at the small table that was really a repurposed workbench with one leg shimmed by a folded coaster. Theo sat with his back to the window. Imani tucked one foot under her and used the edge of naan to wipe up sauce. Eli kept meaning to slow down and kept eating faster, as if someone might clear his plate and send him back to the couch before he had tasted enough.

"This is good," he said.

Theo shrugged. "It's chicken and rice."

"It's good chicken and rice."

Imani pointed her fork at him. "Accept the compliment. We're trying to build healthy pathways in this home."

Theo accepted with a tiny nod and a side glance at Eli that was half joke, half caution. They had become fluent in each other while he was out of frame, and the fact sat heavy at the table.

A phone buzzed on the table. It wasn't his. His own cheap flip phone sat face down and silent, the red voicemail dot still needling the corner of the screen. This was Theo's, faceup by his plate.

Theo read the screen and said, "Manny wants to swap Thursday. Says his kid has croup."

Imani said, "Take it if it gives you Friday night. You said Friday's the chip night."

Theo nodded. "Yeah."

Chip night meant a five-year sobriety chip at the South Grand meeting, then a few guys from Theo's group here for coffee and grocery-store cake. Eli had heard the phrase this morning and guessed poker. He learned better when he reached into the freezer for ice and found three unfrosted sheet cakes stacked on frozen spinach, each with a sticky note in Imani's round handwriting: NOT FOR RANDOM SADNESS.

He swallowed and asked, too late not to sound like an outsider, "Five years this Friday?"

Theo's fork paused halfway to his mouth.

"Yeah," Theo said.

"That's... that's big."

"Yeah," Imani said, softer than before. "We're proud of him."

Theo looked back down. "It's a day."

"It's five years of days," she said.

Silence moved across the table and settled there, dense as steam.

Eli said, "I didn't know the date."

Theo took a breath through his nose and set his fork down. "No. You didn't."

There was no sting in the words. That made it worse. Eli reached for his water glass and found it empty. He stood, filled it from the tap, watched the bubbles turn silver under the kitchen light, and stayed at the sink one beat too long before he sat again.

After dinner he washed dishes because those were the rules: cook, clean, no debate. Theo dried. Imani packed tomorrow's lunches into plastic containers with painter's tape labels and dates written in marker. Their kitchen sounded like domestic percussion, faucet running, drawer slide, snap-lid click, ceramic on rack.

Eli set a glass in the sink too hard and chipped the rim. He sucked air through his teeth.

"My bad."

Theo held out his hand. "Give it here before you fillet your thumb."

Eli gave it over. Theo ran a fingertip around the chip, then put the glass in the trash and wrapped it in a grocery bag so nobody would cut themselves reaching for coffee grounds.

It was a small action, done without speech. It was competence as care.

He dried his hands and went to the hall closet for his duffel because he told himself he needed clean socks. Really he needed to check his work email before midnight because once he slept he would tell himself it could wait one more morning and then one more after that.

In the bedroom they had given him, which was technically Theo's office with a futon and stacked bins of winter coats, the air was cooler. The building's pipes knocked twice inside the wall. Eli sat on the futon edge and opened the old laptop he had left in his trunk for months.

The hospital VPN took three tries. The system gave him a password reset prompt, then a security question, then another prompt. He typed too fast, backspaced, typed again. The screen finally loaded his inbox with the same corporate blue banner he'd looked at every weekday for twelve years. There were forty-two unread messages, most of them automated reminders and one from Cal flagged urgent from three days ago.

He opened Cal's message first.

Subject: Attendance / Required Contact - Final Notice

Cal's writing sounded exactly like his voice: neutral until you learned where the trapdoor sat.
He had stripped this one to a single command and a clock: call before 5:00 p.m. Central or HR would log abandonment under policy 4.7.

The message had been sent at 4:12 p.m. two days ago.

The next message was from Human Resources Shared Services, no name in the signature, only a ticket number.

Subject: Employment Separation Notice

"As of 09/07/2026, your employment with Allegheny Regional Hospital has been terminated under Job Abandonment Policy 4.7."

"Medical, dental, and vision benefits remain active through 09/30/2026."

"Appeal deadline: five business days from notice."

He checked the timestamp. Sent yesterday morning. Appeal deadline: tomorrow at 11:59 p.m. Eastern.

He opened the continuation-rate link anyway. Payroll had taken $242 a month. COBRA for family coverage was $1,186.48.

He wrote the subtraction on the back of an old receipt: $944.48 more every month, starting in three weeks. He wrote it twice because the first time his hand shook and the eight came out as two stacked circles.
He added another number beneath it, 5000 already wired yesterday, and his jaw locked hard enough to pop near his left ear when he swallowed.

The room got narrower, and not metaphorically. The futon mattress edge cut behind his knees. The desk corner pressed his forearm where he leaned. He put both feet flat on the rug and listened to the apartment outside the half-closed door: Theo laughing once at something from the living room, Imani's voice reading from a textbook in a mock-dramatic accent, page turns, then the soft whump of somebody dropping onto the couch.

He could still appeal. He could write a paragraph about family emergency, acute distress, phone loss, route confusion, no charger, whatever pieces might fit. He knew policy language. He could stack terms until they looked reasonable.

He also knew policy 4.7. He had enforced versions of it on people who stopped showing up and thought one good apology email would make payroll rewind.

A sound came out of him before he meant it to, a rough exhale that scraped his throat.

He opened a blank reply to HR, typed three lines, deleted them, typed again:

I request reconsideration based on personal crisis and communication failure.

He stopped. Communication failure sounded like a dropped call, not ten days of disappearance with a burner phone in his duffel.

He deleted it.

He shut the laptop and sat with his hands on his thighs until the skin cooled where the computer had been.

There was a knock on the door frame, though it was not really a knock. Theo tapped twice with his knuckles and stayed in the hall.

"You good?" he asked.

Eli rubbed his face. "No." He looked up. "Come in."

Theo stepped into the room, leaned his shoulder against the bookcase, and waited.

"I got terminated," Eli said. He kept his voice level for the first clause because that was old muscle memory. "As of yesterday. Job abandonment."

Theo did not ask if Eli had misunderstood the email. He did not offer fake shock.

"Okay," he said.

"Insurance runs through end of month. After that it's COBRA at" - Eli gave a short laugh that sounded wrong in his own ears - "it's twelve hundred a month for family coverage."

Theo whistled once under his breath. "Jesus."

"Yeah."

"You telling Mom tonight?"

"I should."

"That's not what I asked."

Eli stared at the stacked storage bins by the futon, each labeled in black marker: WINTER BOOTS, TAXES, CORDS WE MIGHT NEED. "No," he said. "Not tonight. I need to think through what I can actually pay first."

Theo's jaw moved once. "You can think. You can't hide."

"I know."

"Do you?"

The word landed flat. There was no shout and no speech. There was just the question and all the years under it.

Eli said, "I know enough to hear how that sounds." He spread his hands, then dropped them. "I'm not trying to spin you. I'm trying not to call her with half a sentence and no plan."

Theo folded his arms. Ink crossed his forearms, black lines and dates and one faded sparrow Eli had never asked about. "Hours, not days. Appeal clock dies tomorrow night, right?"

"Yeah."

"Are you filing?"

"I don't know if I should."

"Would the appeal be true?"

Eli looked at him and said nothing.

Theo nodded once. "There's your answer. Don't pad it." He pushed off the bookcase. "Come out when you're ready. Imani's making tea."

He left the door open behind him.

Eli stayed seated another minute, maybe three. He could hear water kettle hiss rising in the kitchen. The smell of ginger drifted down the hall where Imani had sliced it with the side of a knife and smashed it before steeping. He knew that because she had shown him at breakfast and said, "If you only slice it, it's polite. If you smash it, it actually helps."

He reopened the laptop, typed a note in his personal draft folder:

Terminated 9/7. Benefits end 9/30. COBRA 1186.48.

Tell Nora tomorrow morning before noon.

No story version.

He shut it again and went to the kitchen.

Imani was at the counter in an old museum T-shirt with a paint stain on the hem, pouring tea through a mesh strainer into three mugs that did not match. She set one in front of him and one in front of Theo and kept her own between both palms for a second before sitting.

"You look like somebody unplugged you," she said.

Eli wrapped his hands around the mug. The ceramic was almost too hot to hold. "Work's done."

Imani did not nod this time. "Don't bring passive voice to my kitchen table. Did you quit, or did they fire you?"

Theo kept his eyes on Eli. "Give it straight."

Heat climbed up Eli's neck. "I got terminated. Job abandonment."

"Done like shift done or done done?" she asked.

"Done done."

She held his gaze another beat, then nodded once. "Okay. That's the sentence."

Theo looked at her, then at Eli. "He got the separation notice. Insurance till month end."

Imani inhaled through her teeth. "Okay. What did HR call it exactly?"

"Job abandonment."

"In writing?"

"Yeah. Second paragraph."

She counted on her fingers as she spoke, looking at Eli directly, not Theo, not the table.

"Tomorrow has two non-negotiables," she said. "You call Nora before noon with the exact wording and the COBRA number. Then you decide on the appeal before the deadline."

She tapped the table with one fingernail. "If you're leaving this week, do you need the hall-drawer charger or the blue cooler? I don't want to wake up to another pride experiment."

Eli almost smiled because the line landed like Imani: practical, a little sharp, and impossible to perform against.

"That's fair," he said.

"I'm not scoring fair," she said. "I'm trying to keep this place from running on panic."

Theo pointed at her mug. "You stole that from me."

"I improved it," she said. "You're welcome."

Theo turned to Eli. "I got something. Hold on."

He disappeared into the bedroom he and Imani shared, came back with a road atlas thick as a brick, spiral binding frayed, cover creased white along the fold. It was a Rand McNally, two editions out of date. Eli recognized it as the one that had lived on the back shelf of Theo's first beater pickup in high school, next to jumper cables and a jug of coolant.

Theo set it on the table and flipped to Missouri. Yellow highlighter marks crossed interstate routes in thin careful strokes. Red pen circles marked towns with notes in block print: DAY LABOR LOT 5AM, CASH DAY SHIFT / WEEKLY MOTEL 42 + TAX / TRUCK STOP W/ SHOWERS.

He turned pages west through Kansas and into eastern Colorado. A corner by Salina had a coffee ring and the faint outline of a phone number written, then scratched out. In the margin near Grand Junction, Theo had taped a narrow strip of paper torn from a receipt with two names and a union local contact.

"I started this in rehab year," Theo said. "Guys would pass route tips around. Where not to sleep, where cops run people off, where the church lot opens early if you keep it clean. I added stuff when I was driving for deliveries after I got out. It's old, but most of it's still close."

Eli touched the page edge. The paper was soft from use. "You carried this around all that time?"

"Yeah."

"I thought you just used your phone."

Theo gave him a look that was not unkind. "Phones die. Service dies. People shut them off when bills hit. Paper keeps working."

Imani sipped tea and said, "There's also a tiny joy in writing on maps like you're planning a heist."

"She color-coded my routes once," Theo said. "Almost got me killed in Omaha because yellow looked like orange at night."

"You're alive and dramatic," she said.

Eli ran his thumb over a penciled note near Denver: IF YOU'RE BROKE, EAT FIRST, THEN PLAN. Under it, smaller, a second line in different handwriting: AND SLEEP. BAD DECISIONS HAPPEN AT 2AM.

"That's yours?" he asked.

Imani shook her head. "Nope. That's a guy named Reggie from Theo's meeting. He writes on everything. Your paper bag from takeout would have life advice by dessert."

Theo closed the atlas halfway and pushed it toward Eli.

"When you go, take it," he said. "Condition is you report in if you use it."

The words caught Eli harder than the termination notice had. He looked up. "You sure?"

"Yes."

"It's yours."

"It's a tool," Theo said. "Tools are for using, not for disappearing. Nine p.m. check-in every night, even if it's just a city name. If your route changes, I need the text before you turn."

Eli swallowed tea that was still too hot and felt it burn down the center of his chest.

"Thanks," he said. "For this. For... all of it."

Theo's eyes stayed on him. "Do not thank me. Do it. Miss one check-in and I send your last route note to Mom myself."

Imani leaned back, cradling her mug, eyes on Eli over the rim of her glasses. "And if you ghost, the hall-drawer charger and blue cooler stay here. We're not funding another vanishing act."

"I hear you."

She tipped her head. "Then give the full sentence."

He did. He did hear it. He heard the promise form in his mouth before the plan did, same as always.

He corrected himself. "No, that's me trying to sound competent. Real sentence: I'll text my city every night by nine. If the route changes, I text before I turn. I call Nora at eight-fifteen with cash on hand, the COBRA number, and the appeal deadline."

Theo nodded. "That's better."

They sat with tea and the atlas open between them like an extra plate at the table.

Later, after dishes and showers and the apartment settling into night sounds, Eli lay on the futon and ran tomorrow in his head like a checklist: wake early, draft bullet points, call Nora, tell facts, no excuses, decide appeal, then pack the car. Through the wall he could hear Theo and Imani in their room, voices low as they traded the dumb details of the day, the museum donor who smelled like mothballs, the forklift horn that stuck on in aisle twelve, whose turn it was to buy detergent.

He remembered Theo at sixteen slamming his bedroom door hard enough to crack the cheap frame. He remembered Theo at twenty-two on a rehab intake call, voice flattened to one syllable answers while Eli stood in a hospital parking lot pretending he could not leave a meeting. He remembered, with nausea that had nowhere to go, how many ordinary facts he had not known when they happened and how quickly you could call that ignorance busyness and get away with it for years.

The atlas sat on the desk by the laptop, a thick square shadow in the dark.

He got up, opened it again under the desk lamp, and copied three notes onto an index card in his blockiest handwriting so he would not claim he could not read it at dawn:

ST. LOUIS -> SALINA -> DENVER CORRIDOR (CHEAP SLEEP OPTIONS)
TEMP AGENCIES / DAY LABOR LOTS (ARRIVE BEFORE 5:30)
CALL HOME BEFORE SPENDING CASH

He stared at the last line. That one was Theo's handwriting in the atlas, not his. The letters leaned forward, impatient.

He set the pen down, left the lamp on, and took the flip phone from his pocket before he could build another alibi.

Nora answered on the fourth ring with monitor chirps behind her and somebody calling for respiratory.

"What is it?"

"It's me," he said. "I got terminated yesterday. Job abandonment. Benefits end September thirtieth. COBRA is eleven-eighty-six-forty-eight for family."

Silence, then a clipped inhale.

"Are you safe right now?" she asked.

"Yeah. At Theo's."

"Good. I can't do this from a med-room doorway." A metal cart clattered in the background. "Call me at eight-fifteen Eastern. Have exact cash on hand, appeal deadline, and where you're sleeping this week. Don't freestyle."

"Okay."

"And Eli?"

"Yeah?"

"Don't disappear before that call."

The line clicked dead.

His stomach seized before he could move. He bent over the desk with both hands braced on the edge and dry-heaved once, then again, acid burning high in his throat.

When the shaking in his forearms settled, he looked down at the index card.

ARRIVE BEFORE 5:30.
8:15 ET CALL NORA.
11:59 PM APPEAL DEADLINE.

If he chased a pre-dawn labor line, he would miss Nora and confirm every accusation in one shot. If he stayed for the call and filed by midnight, he lost the early pickup window and another day of fuel and food he did not have room for.

He carried the atlas to the kitchen table, opened Missouri to Colorado, and left the apartment dark behind him. He did not lie down. He would pay that cost in sleep before sunrise.


# Chapter 7

Eli was still at Theo's kitchen table before sunrise with the atlas spread flat under both forearms, as if the thing might curl up and run if he let it breathe. The cover had worn bald at the corners. The spine crackled every time he pushed it open to the middle. Theo had marked pages with torn strips of painter's tape that looked like little blue tongues, and each strip carried a block-letter label in orange pencil: `CHEAP SLEEP`, `DAY LOT`, `DO NOT STOP HERE AFTER DARK`. Eli kept running his thumb over the groove the pencil had cut in the paper. The groove read less like directions than a record of somebody else's mistakes.

The apartment still held last night's garlic and dish soap. In the next room, a radiator knocked as if someone was tapping a spoon inside it. Theo stood at the stove in a gray T-shirt and work pants, his sleeve tattoos half hidden in steam from the kettle. He had a peppermint toothpick tucked at the corner of his mouth, and he worried it to splinters when he was thinking. He did not look at Eli while he talked.

"I called Darnell on break," he said. "His cousin in Rifle says the framing crews still pick up guys in the lot behind the Sinclair. They pay same day if you don't screw around. You show up at five-thirty, not six. At six you're furniture."

Eli had a pen in his hand and the green folder open by his elbow. The folder held a tuition statement, a mortgage notice, and a printout of the transfer he had made out of the emergency account before he left, fourteen thousand in a clean row of digits that looked less criminal because they were typed. The numbers had not changed when he stared at them. He had $1,186 in cash left in an envelope under the passenger seat and a checking account he did not trust himself to open because each debit line read like a petty confession: gas at 11:42 p.m., motel ice fee, radiator flush, diner tip too high because he did not know what else to do with shame.

"Rifle's almost another thousand miles," he said.

"Nine-sixty if you don't wander." Theo set a mug near him. The coffee was black enough to shine. "You can hit Kansas City before lunch if you get out now. Then it's just highway and bad ideas."

Eli tried a smile and abandoned it. "I'm already in the bad-ideas phase."

Theo pointed with two fingers to the route he'd circled across Missouri and Kansas. "Then use one of mine for a change."

Theo opened the refrigerator, pulled out a sandwich bag, and dropped it by Eli's hand. The bag held two hard-boiled eggs, a packet of salt, and half an apple wrapped in paper towel.

"Imani packed that before she left for the museum," he said. "She said you'll forget to eat when you're panicking. Eat first, then call people."

Eli looked at the bag, then at Theo. "Tell her thanks."

"She wasn't fishing for a kindness speech." Theo snorted through the toothpick. "That was instructions."

Eli turned back to the atlas. Theo's penciled circles hopped west in uneven loops: Columbia, Kansas City, Salina, Hays, Limon, then a heavy ring around Glenwood Springs with `HOTEL LEADS / SPRINGS TRAFFIC` written in the margin. Somebody had once spilled soda on the Colorado pages. The sugar had dried into a faint tack that made the paper drag against his wrist. He traced the line from St. Louis to the mountains and felt his chest sink the way it did in courtrooms and principal offices, rooms where men with clipboards told him what came next and called it choice.

"Call Mom before noon," Theo said. "Don't text. Call."

Eli nodded.

"And don't machine-gun June's phone. One call, maybe two. She'll answer when she wants." Theo finally faced him. His eyes were bloodshot from an overnight shift, but his voice stayed flat, almost gentle. "You don't get to flood her because you're scared. That's not repair. That's pressure."

Eli gripped the pen tighter. "I know."

"You say that a lot."

"I know that too." He looked down at the map because he could not hold Theo's face and his own history in one frame. "Nora said seventy-two hours."

"Then make a plan with a number on it. Not a paragraph." Theo tapped the edge of the folder. "What can you move in seventy-two hours?"

Eli heard himself answer before he had decided whether it was true. "Three grand."

Theo stared. The toothpick clicked against his teeth. "From where?"

"From work. From what I pick up in Colorado."

"That is not seventy-two-hour money and you know it." Theo pulled the toothpick out and snapped it in half. "Don't call her with bullshit math. If you can send four hundred, say four hundred. If you can send zero, say zero. She can survive bad numbers. She can't survive fiction."

Eli wanted to argue, because arguing still gave him a jolt of agency, but nothing came up except a dry sound in his throat. Theo reached into the bowl by the door and handed him the flip phone charger wrapped with black tape where the wire had split.

"You left this in the outlet," Theo said. Then, quieter: "Check your brakes when you fuel up. I heard them whining when you parked."

Eli held the charger, nodded again, and hated himself for how often gratitude and humiliation arrived as the same sensation.

He carried his duffel down the stairs and out to the street where his sedan sat under a sycamore tree shedding bark in curled strips. The trunk smelled like old rubber and laundry detergent from a burst pod he had never cleaned out. He slid the atlas onto the passenger seat, tucked the green folder beside it, and sat for a second with both hands on the wheel before turning the key. The engine caught on the second try and settled into a rough idle. When he pulled away, Theo was at the second-floor window, arms folded, watching the car until it turned the corner.

By the time Eli hit the Poplar Street Bridge, sunlight had climbed onto the steel trusses and made each beam flare white. The Mississippi below looked thick enough to hold tire tracks. At 7:15 Central, Nora's 8:15 Eastern check-in came and went over the river; he let it pass because he still had no number he trusted himself to say out loud. He merged west and called June on speaker with the phone balanced against the dash vent. The ring count rolled to voicemail.

"Hey, June. It's Dad." He tried to keep his voice casual, the tone he used when he asked whether she'd remembered shin guards or a lab notebook. "I'm heading out this morning. I wanted to hear your voice before class if you're up. Call me when you can, okay? No rush. I just... yeah. Love you."

He ended the message and let the silence flood back in. Truck tires drummed the bridge seams. A paper cup rolled in the passenger footwell and hit the seat rail every few seconds like a metronome built for irritation.

At the first fuel stop outside Columbia, he filled the tank to the nearest dollar and watched the numbers climb faster than he could tolerate: 48.00, 49.00, 50.00. The pump clicked off at 51.27. Inside, the station's fluorescent lights flattened everyone's face to the same tired color. A toddler in a dinosaur hoodie slapped both palms on the slush machine and laughed every time his mother pulled him back. Eli bought stale peanut-butter crackers, black coffee that tasted like scorched wire, and a yellow legal pad because the receipt paper in his pocket already had too many false starts on it.

He sat on the curb by the air pump and wrote columns.

`cash on hand`
`fuel`
`food`
`possible deposit`

The math tightened and then collapsed. If he slept in the car and stopped eating like a normal adult and landed work in less than two days, he could maybe send twelve hundred by Thursday. Maybe. The number looked obscene and tiny at the same time.

His phone buzzed.

`June: can't do a call right now. i'm walking into chem.`

Another bubble arrived before he could type.

`please stop calling back-to-back. i heard your message.`

The lowercase, the period at the end of message two and not message one, the way she called him back-to-back when it had been a single call. It all carried the same blunt precision she used as a kid when she caught him cheating at cards and laid the evidence out on the coffee table by suit and number. He typed and erased three replies, then sent: `Okay. I hear you. I'm here when you want.`

He got no response.

Back on the highway, he told himself he would leave it there. Twenty miles later he called again anyway, this time with both hands tight on the wheel and his eyes on the lane paint as if staying centered physically might keep him from slipping verbally.

Her voicemail greeting cut in before the third ring, bright and prerecorded, and he sat up as if she had answered.

"Hi, June, it's me again." His voice went formal without permission. "I'm heading west chasing work leads. You don't have to call back right now. I just wanted to say where I am."

He heard himself sounding like a hospital memo and nearly hung up mid-sentence. "Sorry. That came out weird. Have a good class."

He stabbed the end-call button and laughed once, a single hard bark that startled him more than the cars around him. Then his right tires hit the shoulder rumble strip and screamed under the sedan. He yanked left, heart punching his ribs, and blew past the last signed rest turnout before Kansas City.

Past Boonville, rain swept across the interstate in thin diagonal sheets. Wipers squealed, skipped, caught. A lifted pickup drifted into his lane then corrected. Eli drove with his jaw set and the atlas half open to Missouri on the passenger seat, its page edge fluttering in the fan air like it wanted to turn itself. He could feel the call to Nora waiting in the phone, late now, the way you could feel a tooth you needed pulled.

He took the Kansas City bypass and stopped at a truck plaza where diesel hung in the air like wet wool. The lot was full of idling semis, cabs vibrating in place. Inside, a bank of showers sat behind a counter with handwritten numbers on dry-erase squares. A man in steel-toe boots stood at the microwaves stirring canned chili with a plastic fork. Eli carried his coffee to a corner near the lottery machine and stared at Nora's contact until the screen dimmed.

When he called, she answered on the second ring.

"Where are you?" She did not offer a greeting, and her tone did not even lift at the end.

"Outside Kansas City. Truck stop near the bypass."

"You missed the morning check-in," she said. "So we're doing it now."

He heard a metallic clank through her end, then the muffled rush of air that came when she pushed through a door. Hospital ventilation, he thought. Or maybe a stairwell. She had once told him the only place to cry at work was between floors five and six because the camera there had been broken for years.

"I've got nine minutes," she said. "I start meds at one. Let's do this clean."

Eli swallowed hot coffee and burned his tongue. "Okay."

"I need funds in the joint account within seventy-two hours. Seventy-two from now, not from yesterday's text. Thursday, three p.m. Eastern. If there is no transfer, my attorney files on Friday morning and asks for immediate temporary controls. Do you understand me?"

"Yes."

"Give me the number you can actually clear."

The station's PA squawked something about pump seven. Eli walked outside, into the truck noise, because he could not stand hearing himself lie under fluorescent light.

"Three thousand," he said.

She did not answer right away. The pause was small and absolute.

"From what job?" she asked.

"Day labor in Colorado. Theo has leads in Rifle and Glenwood. Framing crews, cleanup, maybe hotel work. Cash same day if I can get on."

"If."

"Yeah."

"Then what I heard is not a plan. I heard weather and luck." Her voice stayed level, nurse voice, charting voice. "I'm not asking you to explain yourself to me emotionally right now. I'm asking for dates and amounts I can rely on."

"I can send something before Thursday."

"I need a figure I can enter, not a placeholder."

He stared at a puddle stippled with diesel rainbow and watched trucks throw ripples across it as they rolled past. "Twelve hundred by Thursday," he said, each word feeling like a chipped tooth. "Then more once I get work."

"How much more and by when?"

He pressed two fingers into the bridge of his nose. "I don't know yet."

"Then say you don't know. Don't sell me confidence you can't fund." A scrape on her end; maybe she shifted the phone against a locker door. "June has a tuition installment due. Mortgage is already bleeding fees. I can't keep patching this with overtime and grace periods while you improvise a pilgrimage."

The word hit harder than yelling would have.

"I'm not on a pilgrimage," he said.

"Then stop acting like one." For the first time her voice cracked around the edges, then steadied. "Have you talked to her?"

"I called. She doesn't want to talk yet."

"Then stop leaving speeches. It scares her when she hears you sounding..." Nora exhaled. "It sounds like a goodbye every time."

Eli leaned against the cinder-block wall by the ice freezer and felt the cold from inside the machine through his shirt. "I'm trying to keep her in the loop."

"She's eighteen, not your project status report." Nora's words came quicker now. "You two can repair or not repair later. Right now I need money in the account and a text that confirms exact amount. Thursday by three."

"Okay."

"And Eli." She waited until he answered.

"Yeah."

"Don't disappear again if this fails. I can survive broke. I can survive furious. I'm done surviving unknown."

The line clicked dead before he could say anything that would not have sounded like theater.

He stood there with the phone still to his ear while a reefer unit throbbed behind him and spat warm air onto his calves. Somebody laughed by the pumps. A dog barked inside a cab. His coffee had gone cold enough to drink; he swallowed it anyway and tasted paper and burnt metal.

He took out the legal pad and wrote it in one cramped line: `thurs 3pm ET - 1200 minimum; text proof; no story`.

He underlined `no story` twice until the pen tore the page.

An hour west of Kansas City, traffic thinned and the sky opened into a hard blue lid with clouds smeared flat near the horizon. Grain silos rose out of fields like concrete chess pieces. The highway sound settled into one long vowel under the tires. Eli called June again because he had promised himself not to and promises to himself had become the cheapest currency he had.

This time it rang once, then dumped him into the mailbox tone.

"Hey. It's Dad. You don't have to answer. I heard what you said." He kept his eyes on a billboard for fireworks and forced himself to stay plain. "I took fourteen thousand from the emergency account before move-in. That blew a hole in tuition and mortgage the same week and pushed late fees onto the house. You asked me to stop calling and I still kept calling. I'm headed to Colorado for day work, and I'm trying to move twelve hundred by Thursday afternoon. That's all."

He cut himself off before another apology could crawl out and set the phone facedown on the console.

By late afternoon he crossed into Kansas under a state-line sign pocked with dead bugs. He pulled into a rest area outside Topeka where the vending machines hummed against a wall painted with a mural of sunflowers and bison. A retired couple sat on a bench sharing a bag of grapes, each one torn in half first so the other could have the larger side. Eli ate one egg from Imani's bag and read the atlas again.

Theo's handwriting in the margins got tighter the farther west the pages went, as if he had pressed harder trying to force clarity into a map. Next to Limon he had written `WIND WILL KICK YOUR ASS`. Beside a small circle around a town called Parachute: `Ask for union board postings at library.` Near Glenwood Springs: `Motel district / hot springs maintenance / roof crews after hail.` At the top of the page, in smaller print: `If it feels like punishment, do it anyway. Just don't call it purpose.`

Eli stared at that sentence until the letters blurred. Theo had written it for him or for himself years ago; either way Eli inhaled too fast and felt the air snag under his ribs. He shut the atlas, then opened it again before panic could call itself discipline.

His phone buzzed again.

`Theo: Rifle lot's dead this week. Inspector shut pickups down.`

`Theo: If you need cash by Thu, skip north. Go straight to Glenwood motel strip and ask for night cleanup.`

The text punched a hole through the math he'd been nursing all day. Dawn framing money had been his best-case story; motel cleanup would start later and pay less. If he rerouted for Glenwood now, Thursday got tighter. If he chased another lot, Thursday might vanish.

`June: i can't carry road updates between lecture and lab.`

Then another line arrived.

`if you need to text me, ask one thing and wait. i'm not doing six bubbles in class.`

He sat with the message lit in his hand. Ask a question and wait. She was still teaching him, even now.

He typed: `Okay. Are you eating enough?`

He deleted it.

He typed: `What class was today?`

He deleted that one too.

He finally sent: `Do you want me to stop calling for now?`

No answer came after two minutes, then ten, then fifteen. He watched a thunderhead build in the distance and throw shadow over the parking lot.

When he got back on the road, he kept the phone face down on the passenger seat and did not touch it for almost an hour. Then it vibrated once, skittering toward the atlas.

`June: yes, for now. no calls. if the thursday number changes, send one text with the new number.`

The message lodged in his throat, a hard swelling that made swallowing hurt. He called anyway before his better self could intervene, thumb slick enough to skid across the screen. Her phone dumped him straight to mailbox without a ring.

"June, it's Dad." His voice cracked on the first word. "I read what you said and I still hit call. I keep doing exactly what you asked me not to do." He dragged air through clenched teeth and heard it whistle. "I'll text only. I hear you. If the money shifts or the timeline changes, I'll send numbers. Nothing else."

The mailbox tone cut out, and he tasted blood where he had bitten the inside of his cheek. He smacked the heel of his hand against the steering wheel once, hard enough to sting. It was not rage, not exactly. The hit was his body trying to force a stuck gear into place.

Dusk gathered in layers, orange leaking down behind feedlots and water towers, then gray taking everything back. He passed a sign for a motel with WEEKLY RATES painted in red on white plywood and considered stopping. If he slept six hours he could still reach eastern Colorado by late morning and maybe make Glenwood by evening for cleanup leads. Any detour would burn the margin he had left. So would driving tired, but he chose the margin anyway. He took the exit, rolled to the stop sign, then turned right back onto the interstate without looking at the motel office.

Motion still felt safer than rooms.

By nine-thirty he was west of Salina with truck traffic rising again, convoys of headlights and spray. The radio gave weather in clipped county names and warned of high crosswinds along I-70 into Colorado. Eli drove with the window cracked so cold air could keep him sharp. It smelled of cut hay, hot asphalt, and diesel. His lower back ached. The old stiffness in his right knee throbbed every time he eased off the gas.
