A voice in the background called Theo's name.

"Got to go," Theo said. "Eat something with salt before boarding. Your voice sounds like shit."

"Love you," Eli said, the words out before he could decide whether he deserved them.

Theo took one beat and answered, "Love you too. Keep moving the right direction this time."

The line clicked dead.

Eli kept the dead screen pressed to his thigh until the plastic warmed under his palm. Then he opened a blank note and typed Theo's sequence exactly: **7:30 clipboard. First shift. Receipt text same day. Call before last ten.** His jaw hurt from clenching. The list felt like handcuffs and like oxygen at the same time. Miss one step, and Tuesday turned into another apology call with no money behind it.

He bought a packet of salted peanuts for two dollars and ate them slowly. The salt cut through the metallic taste in his mouth. He took two ibuprofen from a travel bottle and drank the rest of his water. His rib stayed loud anyway.

At ten the boarding area crowded fast with blankets, neck pillows, paper sacks from fast-food counters, plastic grocery bags tied in knots, and one cardboard cat carrier with key-poked air holes.

Eli opened the folder on his knees and checked the stack by touch: wire receipt dry in the sleeve, bus ticket folded to the barcode, insurance accounting flattened behind the tuition statement so no corner stuck out.

He had become a man who traveled with paper proof of each small step, the way probationers carried stamped cards. He understood the humiliation and the use. If he lost the feeling, he still had the facts.

When boarding began, Sandeep scanned tickets and sent people down the ramp in a steady rhythm. As Eli reached the scanner, Sandeep glanced at his face and asked quietly, "You ate?"

"Enough," Eli said.

Sandeep nodded once, like that was the answer he expected from men in Eli's condition. "Seat 18A, left side. Outlet is loose; hold the plug up with your shoe."

The bus held stale coffee, vinyl heat, and a bleachy swipe from whatever cleaner they used between runs when they had time to use cleaner at all. Eli stowed the duffel overhead and took the window seat. The glass was cold enough to sting when he leaned his temple against it.

Outside, terminal lights smeared across the pane in yellow streaks.

Eli took out the wire receipt, unfolded it, and read Nora's name printed in block capitals above the amount. One hundred fifty dollars. Too small to fix anything. Large enough to remove one excuse.

The bus merged onto the freeway. Tire noise settled into a steady hum, low and relentless, like a machine set to keep counting whether he watched it or not. He slid the receipt back into the folder, zipped the folder shut, and looped the strap around his forearm the way the city driver had told him.

He watched Utah thin into black desert and kept his hand on the folder until his fingers cramped.


# Chapter 14

Sleep never came on the ride in. The bus dropped him on East Fourth under a sky the color of rubbed nickel, with wind coming off the desert carrying grit. Eli stood on the curb with the green document folder under his arm and his duffel between his shoes, counting how many breaths he could take before the left side of his chest pinched. Four before it pinched. Five if he held his shoulders still and lied to himself.

The casino hiring office sat behind the parking garage instead of inside the building where guests saw chandeliers and mirrored escalators. Out back there were loading docks, dented linen carts, and a row of smokers in maintenance jackets passing a lighter down the line like a relay baton. The laundry intake door opened and shut every few seconds, and each time it opened he got a burst of wet heat and detergent so strong it tasted chalky.

A woman in a burgundy security polo took his ID, glanced at the Pennsylvania address, and clipped a paper badge to his shirt that said VISITOR in red block letters. She had a tiny silver hoop through the right side of her nose and chewed cinnamon gum with the tired determination of somebody staying awake on purpose.

"You're for laundry?" she asked.

"Whatever starts fastest."

She snorted once, not meanly. "That answer always means laundry."

She pointed him toward a trailer office with fake wood paneling and a hand-lettered sign that said EMPLOYMENT. The trailer shook each time a truck hit the dock bumpers outside, and each shudder lit up the old crack under his left ribs. Inside, the air conditioner rattled loud enough to compete with the slot-machine spill leaking through the wall.

The woman behind the desk had already split his forms into two piles before he sat: usable now and fix later. "Tasha Bell," she said, still typing. "If this packet misses cutoff, payroll punts you a week." Her hair was pulled into a low braid held with a pencil, and there was a half-finished crossword next to her keyboard with three squares darkened in hard pen because she'd changed her mind and pressed too deep. She wore rhinestone cat-eye readers on a beaded chain and kept pushing them up to the top of her head every time she looked at his papers, then dragging them back down for the next line. The old compliance reflex slid into place in him anyway: answer fast, look steady, never admit how thin the paperwork really was.

"You've got out-of-state ID, no local lease, and your social on a sticky note." She tapped the note with a fingernail. "Please tell me you've got the card."

"I lost it in the crash. I can get a replacement sent."

"Okay." She clicked through screens. "Background and E-Verify can still clear with the number if it matches. If it doesn't, there's no fix-it-later window. They cut you. You understand?" The word cut landed in his gut harder than it should have. He had used it himself for years when someone else's job was on the line.

He pressed his thumb against the edge of the desk until the nail blanched.

She looked up at him long enough to make him answer with words.

"I understand."

"Good. Don't coast through this one on yeses."

She spun the monitor toward him and walked him through two different entities with two different logos: Silver Sage Resort for night-audit coverage and Sierra Textile Services for laundry. Same building, same elevators, separate payrolls, separate supervisors, separate ways to avoid paying overtime on the same exhausted body. He read every line because he still had the old habit of reading contracts, and he still heard Cal Bristow in his head saying You can either know what you signed or you can explain later why you didn't.

Tasha watched him read, arms folded. "Most people autograph first and panic after. You're either careful or buying time."

"Careful."

"Careful costs you shifts around here."

She said it like a weather report.

The laundry manager came in before he finished the tax form. Her name tag said DEENA L., and she wore black work gloves even indoors, the fingertips dusted pale from lint and bleach. A pink inhaler peeked out of her cargo pocket every time she sat down. She did not sit down now. She stood behind his chair, scanning him like she was deciding whether his spine would hold up under cart weight.

"How old are you?" Deena asked.

"Fifty-two."

She made a face like she was tasting pennies. "Any lifting restriction?"

He felt the rib flash hot when he inhaled. "No."

Tasha gave him one quick side look, then went back to the screen.

Deena continued. "Graveyard. Ten p.m. to six-thirty on the laundry floor. If audit's short, I loan you upstairs till ten-thirty. We run on pounds and minutes, not intentions. Miss in your first two weeks and I strip your locker by lunch. Step off line without radioing and I reassign your station before your cart cools. Bring me doctor's-note drama before ninety days and I'll swap you for the next warm body."

There was no edge to her voice. That was the edge.

Eli asked, "What's the rate?"

"Laundry is fourteen twenty-five. Audit support is sixteen ten. First check holds an eighty-dollar uniform and shoe deduction. Temporary badge until paperwork clears. Badge doesn't clear, your last week sits in review."

"Sits where?"

"In review." Deena lifted one shoulder. "Could be two days. Could be three pay cycles. Depends who's mad."

Tasha snapped her gum and kept typing.

Outside, something metal hit concrete and clanged twice before it settled. Eli looked at the numbers on the sheet again. Fourteen twenty-five. Sixteen ten. Four doubles and even one short laundry swing would put him in range to send real money, not apology money. If he did not get that much, he stayed in motion and called it progress while everyone else paid for it.

He signed.

Deena slid a locker key across the desk. "You start tonight. Bring ibuprofen if you take it. We don't stock meds because legal won't let us."

"I've got some."

"You'll need more."

She turned to go, then looked back. "And don't call me ma'am. Makes me feel dead."

---

The laundry floor lived in permanent midnight no matter what the clock said. No windows, only sodium lights and strip fluorescents that made everybody's skin look waxy and tired. Conveyor belts carried sheets overhead in gray loops. Industrial washers thumped with a bass note he felt in his sternum before he heard it. Steam rolled out each time the press doors opened, and the heat sat on his shoulders like wet wool.

Mateo Rivas showed him the sort line and did not waste words. He was maybe thirty, maybe younger in the face and older in the hands. He had a tiny tattoo of a sparrow at his wrist and a white athletic tape wrap over his left thumb, refreshed so cleanly it looked like ritual. When he opened his locker to stash his lunch, Eli saw a crayon drawing taped inside the door: three stick figures under a huge yellow sun and the word DAD in crooked block letters.

Mateo caught him looking and grinned without embarrassment. "My kid says that's me in the middle. I told her I don't have that much hair."

"You probably had more hair in her version."

"Everybody's got more hair in her version."

He handed Eli earplugs from a box mounted to the wall. "Use these or go deaf in installments."

Deena ran orientation by walking backward in front of them, yelling over the machines with one hand on her inhaler like a talisman. "Whites and sheets on line one. Towels on line two. Blood bins flagged red, chemicals flagged yellow. Don't play hero with needles. You see sharps, you stop and call me. If you get stuck and don't report it, I write you up and you get tested. No exceptions."

Another worker muscled past hard enough to clip Eli's hip with a linen cart, then shouted "Behind!" after she had already moved through him. She did not slow down. Her name patch read JENNY as she dumped fresh folded towels onto line two, and someone had stitched a tiny smiling cactus above the J with green thread, the careful kind of handwork that had to be done at a kitchen table by lamplight.

By midnight Eli's shirt stuck to his spine. By one his left side began sending sharp narrow warnings every time he lifted a wet sheet bundle from the washer mouth to the feed table. The cracked rib had felt almost civil in bus seats and short shifts. It turned mean in heat.

He adjusted by using his right side more, then overcorrected and yanked his old knee when a wheel on the cart caught in a floor seam. Pain moved through him in layered bands, not dramatic, just relentless and cumulative, each band collecting until he stopped being able to pretend they were separate problems.

Mateo noticed the hitch in his step on their first break. They stood in a cinder-block room with two vending machines, a dead ficus in the corner, and a mural of Lake Tahoe sunrises painted directly over a crack that split the wall from ceiling to tile.

"You move like my tio after roofing," Mateo said. "You busted up?"

"I was in a car wreck a month ago."

"And you took laundry. Jesus." Mateo cracked open a can of peach energy drink and took two fast swallows. "You got kids?"

"Two."

Mateo tipped the energy-drink can toward the locker-room door. "I do this because my ex and I switch daycare every six months like it's a hostage exchange. You?"

Eli looked at his hands. The skin between his thumb and forefinger had gone chalky from detergent splash and dried out enough to sting.

"I owe money I shouldn't have touched."

Mateo absorbed that without asking for the story. "Yeah. Welcome to the building."

When break ended, Deena caught Eli swallowing two ibuprofen dry and said, "Take four next time."

"Label says two."

"Label isn't pushing carts for you."

At three forty the audit supervisor came down because crossover was short and recon could not wait. Her name was Kendra Shaw. She pointed her metal clipboard at him before she reached him, already assigning his lane. She wore a black cardigan over uniform slacks and shoes with tiny silver cats embroidered on the toes, the kind of hidden detail people gave themselves when the rest of life had to be plain. Her travel mug smelled like cardamom.

She skimmed his temporary badge and frowned. "You're the provisional?"

"Yeah."

"Keep your hands off anything guest-facing. You're back-office recon. Folio and cage envelope have to reconcile before I call finance. You see a variance, you flag me before it leaves the room. Bury one and your access dies before sunrise. And when your body's about to give out, tell me before you hit tile. I need clean numbers, not a workers-comp incident."

"Got it."

Kendra looked at him again, maybe at the sweat-dried salt on his collar, maybe at the stiffness he was no longer hiding well. "You got reading glasses?"

"No."

She slid a pair of cheap +1.25 readers from the pocket of her clipboard and set them on top of the folio stack. "Use mine. Bring them back. I only lend these to people who hand them back."

The recon room felt like another planet after laundry: overcooled, quiet except for printer chatter and the hum of fluorescent ballast. He sat at a steel table and matched cage envelopes to folio summaries while Kendra keyed corrections into a terminal that still ran a green-text interface older than June. The work should have felt easier because it was seated and clean. Instead his body shook when it finally stopped lifting, and his rib throbbed in time with the printer.

At nine twelve he misread a seven as a one and Kendra caught it before he finalized.

"You're drifting," she said.

"I'm fine."

"No, you're not. And I can't file 'I'm fine' when finance asks why a variance almost posted. Tell me what failed."

He stared at the line item, then told the truth in the smallest possible shape. "Left side pain. I'm compensating and losing focus."

"You're initialing a coaching slip." She pulled a yellow form from her clipboard, wrote in tight block letters, and pushed it toward him. "Provisional recon error plus pain disclosure puts you on laundry-only for tomorrow's crossover. Two clean laundry shifts, no variance noise, and I'll review you back into audit."

He initialed.

"I'll take the two shifts."

When he gave her back the borrowed readers at ten thirty, she said, "Deena asked if you can cover four doubles and a five-hour laundry swing this week. I approved the hours, but you're still laundry-only until that hold clears."

He thought about Marisol in the Glenwood Springs motel bathroom: his palm on the sink, his mouth on the burn mark on her forearm, Nora's name buzzing on his phone until the screen went dark. Marisol had asked whether Nora knew, and he had said, "Not yet," like delay was an answer instead of a choice. While he kept that lie warm, Nora covered the mortgage and June lived on a cut meal plan.

"I can stay upright."

Kendra's expression did not soften. "Then prove it. Bring me two clean nights and I take the hold off."

---

By the fourth night he learned where to stand to catch the only cross-breeze on the floor, the two-second strip near the receiving door where outside air slipped in when trucks backed to dock three. He learned Jenny sang old nineties country under her breath when she sorted stains, and that Deena took exactly thirty-seven steps from her desk to the press line because she counted when her asthma got bad. Mateo rubbed a quarter between finger and thumb before every call to his daughter, not for luck exactly, more like friction to keep himself in place.

Pain had hierarchies. The rib was bright and sharp when he lifted. The knee was dull and mean when he pushed. The palms burned from hot damp cotton sliding faster than his skin wanted to allow. By sunrise each day all three merged into one low electrical field, and he stopped assigning names to parts.

On Thursday, Tasha called him into the employment trailer after shift and closed the door.

"E-Verify kicked a tentative nonconfirmation," she said. "Birth year mismatch."

He felt the room shrink around the word nonconfirmation.

"I entered what was on your I-9," she added. "Could be a keystroke. Could be your old record from when you used Ian for that prepaid account and then forgot where else you used it. People do dumb things when they're trying to disappear."

His face warmed, then cooled. "What happens now?"

"I filed the contest notice. You've got eight federal workdays, and the clock started when I printed this. Until Social Security clears it, you're a conditional hire. Conditional hire means managers can trim or move your schedule and call it operational necessity." She tapped the desk. "Deena can build that paper trail in her sleep."

"So she can cut me."

"She can cut you, park a check in review, or bounce you between entities, as long as the paperwork reads clean."

Outside, the dock alarm beeped three slow notes as a trailer reversed. Tasha slid a printed form across the desk, along with a pen chewed nearly in half.

"Sign here acknowledging notice."

He signed.

She watched him cap the pen and added, almost gently, "You don't have to tell me your story. But if you're trying to send money somewhere fast, conditional status is blood in the water. They'll work you to wire and then call it operational necessity."

He folded the notice into the green folder and felt the paper edge catch a small cut on his thumb. "Then I take the hours while I've got them."

"That's one strategy."

"What's the other strategy?"

"Keep enough spine to walk when they start stealing twice."

He left without answering because he did not know which theft she meant.

Deena posted the weekend schedule ten minutes later. Next to his name: LAUNDRY 2200-0630 / AUDIT 0630-1030, Friday through Monday, plus a laundry-only swing Tuesday 2200-0300. Four doubles and a five-hour fifth night. He stared at the sheet until Mateo shoulder-bumped him.

"They gave you the punishment tour," Mateo said.

"Looks like it."

Mateo whistled through his teeth. "You either pissed her off, or she likes your work, which here is the same thing."

At midnight, halfway through loading king-size sheets into the tunnel washer, Eli's rib seized so hard his left hand opened on reflex and dumped half a load onto the wet concrete. Deena appeared at his side before he could bend.

"Stop." She keyed her radio. "Line one needs a mop and a hazard sign." Then to him, quieter: "Badge."

He blinked. "What?"

"Badge. No crossover tonight." She peeled the temporary audit sticker off his plastic and pocketed it. "I logged a safety drop and sent Kendra the incident. You're laundry-only until she clears you."

"I'm still standing."

"You dropped sixty pounds of wet linen."

"It slipped."

She held his gaze for one long beat. "You want audit access back, tighten up. I can't babysit you."

She walked away before the mop crew arrived.

He finished the load with his right arm shaking and his breath sliced into shallow pieces. On break he sat on an overturned milk crate behind the dock because the break room lights made his eyes buzz. The desert air was cold enough to sting the sweat on his neck. He took his shirt hem in both hands and pressed fabric hard against his left side until the spasm eased.

The flip phone vibrated in his pocket. Unknown number. He answered on the second buzz.

"Theo," the voice said. "Don't get dramatic, it's me. I'm on a borrowed line because mine's dead again."

Eli straightened instinctively though nobody could see him. "Hey."

"I got your text with the hours screenshot. That's real money if they keep you."

"If they keep me."

"You sound wrecked."

"I'm working."

"No shit. Listen. If you send anything this week, send it with a memo line Nora can track. Don't do anonymous cash apps and then expect trust points."

Eli let a truck engine idle fill the gap before answering. "I wasn't planning anonymous."

"Good. Also, June asked if you'd called me." Theo paused. "I said yeah, because you had. She didn't ask anything else."

The words hit his cracked side like a cart handle, knocking half a breath out of him. June asked if you'd called me. Not How are you, not Where are you exactly. Just a systems check.

"Thanks," Eli said.

"Don't thank me. Just keep receipts. And don't die in a laundry room trying to sprint through fourteen grand in two weeks."

"I won't."

Theo gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "You don't get to promise that to me."

The line clicked dead. Borrowed phone, borrowed minutes, son setting terms like a foreman assigning safety tasks. Eli sat in the dark until Deena's voice came over the dock speaker calling all hands back to line one.

---

Saturday bled into Sunday without a clean border. He measured time in machine cycles and audit print runs, in how many ibuprofen remained in the orange bottle, in whether the left side caught at four a.m. or held until six. He started taping his rib before shift with cloth athletic tape from the casino gift shop because the pharmacy had closed when he got off. He applied it in the locker room mirror with his shirt off, watching his hands work around a bruise blooming yellow at the edges and violet at the center.

Mateo came in one morning while Eli was rewrapping and leaned against the lockers, unwrapping a breakfast burrito from foil. "You ever think about just quitting and doing something your body can survive?"

"Every hour."

"So why don't you."

Eli smoothed the tape and pulled his shirt down. "Because surviving is not the same problem as now."

Mateo chewed, swallowed, and lifted two fingers in a tired salute. "Fair."

Then, after a beat: "My girl hates dinosaurs. Everybody thinks kids love dinosaurs. She says they're just birds with anger issues. That's why I work this shift, by the way. Daycare's cheaper if I drop her before six."

It was such a specific opinion that Eli smiled before he meant to.

"Birds with anger issues."

"Exactly. She's six and already funnier than me."

They clocked in.

By Sunday night, after two laundry-only shifts and a signed clean-floor log from Deena, Kendra gave him back a temporary audit sticker and began handing him more complex reconciliations without comment: group booking adjustments, disputed minibar charges, count-room slips that did not match the nightly revenue summary. He moved slower than he wanted and still faster than the temp before him, according to Kendra, who finally said on Monday, "You're useful enough that I argued to keep your hours even with the paperwork flag."

"Why?"

She shrugged, eyes on the screen. "Because I hate retraining people. And because you don't bullshit me when you're wrong."

A printer jammed. She opened the panel and tugged out a wrinkled strip of receipt paper with two blackened fingertips. "Don't confuse that with friendship."

"I don't."

"Good. I just put you down as primary overflow for weekend recon. That means extra audit coverage after laundry when someone no-shows. Better rate, worse spine. Decline one call and I pull your recon access; you're laundry-only until I can train another closer."

His first full check posted Tuesday night. Gross: $813.35. Deductions bit hard: taxes, uniform, shoe holdback, cafeteria charge he did not remember making and would have to dispute later. Net: $603.18.

He stood by the employee kiosk while slot jackpots chimed through the wall in fake celebration. His forearms still trembled from the tunnel washers, and this check was enough to hit Nora's account like proof he wasn't just apologizing by phone anymore.

He bought a money order in the twenty-four-hour pharmacy and sent five hundred to Nora with the memo line Theo had told him to use: MORTGAGE ARREARS - OCT WK 2. The clerk behind the bulletproof glass counted the bills twice, then made him read the memo line back before she printed the slip. She wore a University of Nevada hoodie and had silver glitter on her eyelids that caught the fluorescent light every time she blinked. Then she slid the receipt under the slot.

"Keep the stub," she said. "People always lose the stub."

"I won't."

He tucked it into the green folder behind June's tuition statement and felt a narrow strip of steadiness, the kind that held for one minute before the ground moved again.

When he reached the motel room he rented by the week, the carpet smelled of old smoke and lemon cleaner fighting to a draw. He sat on the edge of the bed, shoes still on, and opened June's contact thread on the flip phone. Three outgoing calls from the past month, one answered for twenty seconds before she said she had to run, then nothing except his voicemails stacked like unsold inventory.

He looked at the time. 11:47 p.m. In Pittsburgh it would be 2:47 a.m., but June lived by campus hours and library shifts now, strange pockets of sleep and work he no longer understood. He could wait and call at a civilized time. He could overthink until the next shift started.

He called.

It rang five times and dropped to her voicemail. The greeting was simple and brisk: "It's June. Leave it."

He held the phone tight enough the plastic creaked.

He saw the Glenwood motel mirror fogged white, Marisol's braid dark against his wrist while Nora's name flashed on his screen and rang out unanswered.

"Hey, Junie, it's Dad." He stopped, reset. "It's me. I'm in Reno, doing casino laundry and night-audit support. I sent five hundred tonight with your mom's name in the memo so she can track it. I lied to your mother from a motel bathroom in Glenwood, and while I hid there Nora carried the mortgage and you rationed meal swipes."

A siren wailed two streets over and faded.

He continued, voice rough from steam and lint. "I'm ashamed of what I did to both of you. I miss you every day. If your meal plan's still cut, text me the gap and I'll send it."

"You don't have to call me back. Good night."

He ended the message and sat with the dark screen reflected in the motel window. Across the parking lot, a man in pajama pants smoked under the ice machine and watched the empty road like he expected someone to pull in and save him from his own choices.
He did not hit replay. Hearing himself say ashamed had already scraped him raw.

Eli set the phone facedown, then picked it up again two minutes later to check for a response he knew would not be there. Nothing. He set an alarm for 8:30 p.m. and lay back without undressing, one hand over the taped rib as if he could hold the bone together by pressure alone.

The room heater kicked on and rattled like loose dice in a cup. He closed his eyes and pictured Nora opening the transfer receipt, then June deleting his voicemail before he got to the end. Tomorrow meant another shift he could not miss, another night of lint in his lungs and pain taped tight to his ribs. None of it comforted him. It only kept him pointed east.

At 1:16 a.m., before he slept, he checked the phone again.

No new voicemail. No text. No missed call.

At 8:30 p.m. the alarm dragged him upright into the same ache, the same lint smell still trapped in his hair, the same badge waiting on the table by the door. Deena had texted the shift line at noon: I NEED YOU FOR EXTRA AUDIT COVERAGE AFTER LAUNDRY. PLEASE CONFIRM. The laundry-only hold was off.

He typed back with his thumb.

`I'll be there.`

He clipped the badge to his shirt and left before the ache could talk him out of it, heading for the laundry corridor where the load cards waited above Dryer Three.


# Chapter 15
The load card above Dryer Three listed king sheets, forty-two pounds, and no bleach. Eli read it only after he pressed his left hand to the warm steel and judged the heat for himself. At one-forty in the morning, the laundry corridor under the casino sounded like weather trapped in a pipe: washers banging into high spin, ductwork answering with a deep metallic moan that ran the length of the room.

His rib caught when he bent for the lint trap. The old crack on his left side did not stab anymore, but it still gripped him if he twisted wrong, a hard wire under the skin that yanked his breath short. He waited it out, one palm on his thigh, then stood and shut the trap with his forearm. Keep earning. Keep moving. Keep his head clear enough to send money east on Friday and another piece on Tuesday. He had texted Deena he'd cover extra audit after laundry; the night had no soft edge left in it. If he treated every shift like numbers on a page, he could stay in front of shame for another week.

Mateo Rivas rolled a linen cart past him and whistled the same four bars from a norteno song he had never finished. He always stopped before the melody turned sad. The glitter bracelet on his wrist flashed in the fluorescent light, cheap pink plastic beads with SOFIA spelled in silver letters. His daughter had made it at school, he had said on Eli's first night, and he wore it even when the floor supervisor called it unsanitary and told him to cut it off.

"You're stacking those too high," Mateo said, nodding at Eli's cart. "You hit that door frame again, Ms. Kline's gonna act like it's an assault on corporate property."

"Then you'll have to testify for me," Eli said.

Mateo grinned and bared the chipped incisor he kept promising to fix when overtime finally stuck. "For twenty bucks and a coffee, I'm your star witness."

They moved in parallel for the next hour, two men in gray uniforms with their names heat-pressed above the pocket, passing each other at the ironer and the supply cage without speaking much. Eli liked Mateo because he talked in useful specifics and then shut up. He did not ask where Eli came from. He did not perform concern. He liked to fold pillowcases into perfect squares and line them by size, because that small order mattered to him.

At two-thirty-five, Eli crossed to the audit desk upstairs to cover the hour between slot counts and room reconciliations. The casino floor had thinned to insomniac regulars and men who had lost enough money that they played slower, trying to reverse arithmetic by force of care. He keyed in room charges from handwritten slips, checked signatures against ID scans, and printed a folio for a drunk bachelor party that had tried to expense three trays of wings to a room already checked out. The front glass doors breathed cold each time they opened. Outside, Reno wind dragged grit across the parking lot in long, dry scratches.

Mateo came up once for guest towels and stood beside Eli's chair longer than needed, rubbing his sternum through his shirt.

"You all right?" Eli asked.

"Heartburn," Mateo said. "That burrito from the machine was a felony."

"Take ten." Eli tapped the clock in the corner of his monitor. "We have room."

"If Kline catches me sitting, she'll write me like a parking ticket." Mateo gave Eli a look that tried to make it a joke. It missed by a fraction. "I'll hit the men's room and get back down."

He went through the service door and disappeared toward the employee hallway.

Eli kept entering charges. At 2:41 and again at 2:44, he glanced at the clock and kept entering charges. He heard the floor shift in layers: slot chimes, a woman laughing too hard near blackjack, vacuum motors from housekeeping doing a late pass under the lobby chairs. At 2:49, Reina from housekeeping came around the corner with a roll of trash liners tucked under her arm and a smear of blue disinfectant on one cheek.

"You saw Mateo?" she asked.

Eli looked up. "He went to the restroom."

"He's in there too long." She bounced once on her heels, not from impatience but from the way she always moved when worried, quick weight shifts like a boxer between rounds. "I knocked twice. No answer."

The clock in Eli's screen ticked to 2:50. He stood too fast, felt the rib catch, and waited half a breath before following her through the service corridor.

The employee restroom sat behind a coded door near the break room, where the smell of fryer oil mixed with industrial lemon cleaner. The light inside hummed. One stall door was shut. Under it, Mateo's shoes pointed at the tile, toes inward.

"Mateo," Eli said, knuckles to the metal partition. "Hey. You with me?"

Nothing.

He knocked harder. The door rattled but the lock held.

Reina was already backing up, hand on her radio. "I call security."

"Call 911 first." Eli kept his voice flat because flat voices made other people move. "Tell them possible overdose, male, thirties, not responsive, employee restroom near south service elevator." He hit the door with his shoulder. Pain flared along his left side and down his back, bright enough to blur the edges of the sink. The latch gave halfway but jammed against dead weight.

He shoved again. The door opened four inches. He rammed it once more and got twelve. Mateo lay wedged between the toilet and the partition, knees folded under him, cheek against the tile. His skin had the gray cast of paper left in rain. His lips were blue at the center.

Eli dropped beside him. "Mateo. Open your eyes." He pressed two fingers against Mateo's neck and felt for pulse through sweat and stubble and his own shaking hand. There was something, a thin flutter that vanished when he pressed too hard. He eased up and it came back, slow.

Reina's voice snapped over the radio in rapid English and Spanish, then she looked at Eli. "Ambulance coming. Security says they're bringing the kit."

"Good. Unlock that outer door and keep people out." Eli tipped Mateo's head back and lifted his chin. A sour chemical smell sat under the lemon cleaner. There was residue on the sink edge, white streaks dragged thin with water. Mateo's chest barely rose.

Eli put his ear near Mateo's mouth. There was faint air, and it was not enough.

He had done mock drills for years in conference rooms with carpet squares and laminated response cards while people checked email under the table. This was not that. Mateo's hand was cooling fast against the tile. The SOFIA bracelet had twisted to the inside of his wrist, beads digging into skin.

"Come on," Eli said, pitching his voice low so it might reach him before the words did.

Boots slapped in the hallway. Darryl from security came in with a red overdose pouch, breath sawing hard enough to wake a faint whistle in his hearing aid. He dropped to his knees before the radio chatter finished.

"Naloxone," Darryl said, already kneeling. "You got respirations?"

"Slow. Pulse is there but weak."

"Give one now." Darryl ripped the plastic open and shoved the spray into Eli's hand. "Tilt his head. Full press."

Eli slid the nozzle into Mateo's right nostril and pressed until it clicked. He counted out loud because counting kept panic from grabbing the wheel.

"One thousand one. One thousand two."

Nothing changed. Mateo's jaw stayed slack, breath still shallow and far apart.

"Mask," Darryl said.

Reina handed over the pocket mask from the wall first-aid box with hands that shook only at the fingertips. Eli sealed it over Mateo's mouth and nose and gave two slow breaths, watching for chest rise. The first barely moved him. The second lifted more.

"Again," Darryl said, tapping the second hand on his taped watch. "Two minutes. If he doesn't wake, second dose."

Eli breathed for Mateo, steady and deliberate, forcing himself not to rush. The tile dug through his pant leg into his knee. His left rib burned each time he leaned forward, and sweat ran down his spine under the uniform shirt. The hallway noise narrowed to this room, this chest that would not decide whether to keep working.

A memory cut in without warning: Theo at nineteen on the basement futon in Pittsburgh, eyes half-open and unfocused, mumbling that he was fine while his speech smeared together. Eli had stood in the doorway with his briefcase still in his hand, telling himself it was probably weed, probably nothing, because he had an early audit and no room left in the day for catastrophe. Nora had found the pill bottle an hour later, with an orange cap and no label.

"Eli." Darryl's voice pulled him back. "Second dose."

Eli switched nostrils and pressed again.

Mateo jerked once, a full-body shudder that knocked his heel against the stall base. For one second Eli thought that was it, a recovery script exactly how the training video sold it. Then Mateo retched hard, thick fluid and bile across the tile and Eli's sleeve. His eyes flew open but did not focus.

"Roll him. Roll him now." Darryl and Eli turned him onto his side, clearing his airway while Mateo coughed wetly and tried to push them off with weak, angry motions.

"Easy," Eli said. "You're okay. Stay on your side."

"Don't," Mateo rasped, then coughed again so hard his shoulders shook. "Don't call my mom."

"Nobody's calling anybody yet," Darryl said, firm as a drill sergeant and gentler than his words. "You breathe. That's your only job."

Sirens rose outside and cut at the curb. Two paramedics came in with a stretcher and monitor bag, moving fast but not frantic. The woman at the head introduced herself as Nia, a crescent-moon tattoo tucked behind her left ear, and dropped to Mateo's side without looking away from his chest. Her partner clipped a pulse oximeter to Mateo's finger and called out numbers that made Eli's stomach go cold.

"Sat seventy-nine. Resp six and shallow."

Nia looked at Eli. "What did he take?"

"Unknown. Found down in stall around two-fifty. Two intranasal naloxone doses, about three minutes apart. Rescue breaths with barrier mask."

"Any trauma?"

"No head strike that I saw."

She nodded once, all business, then spoke to Mateo in a voice meant to cut through fog. "Mateo, listen. You're at Silver Mesa. You overdosed. We're putting oxygen on you and taking you in."

Mateo tried to sit up. Nia put a hand to his shoulder and kept him down with controlled pressure.

"Sofia," he said, or maybe only the first syllable.

The bracelet glittered again when he reached for air.

The medics loaded him, secured straps, asked Darryl for camera timestamps, and wheeled him out through the service corridor. The whole thing took nine minutes. It felt like one long inhalation that never turned into exhale.

Eli stayed on one knee after the stretcher cleared the hallway, hand braced on wet tile, waiting for his pulse to come down. It did not. When he pushed himself up, the room tipped and he caught the sink with his forearm hard enough to jar the rib. He bent over the toilet and dry-heaved until his throat burned.

When he straightened, Mateo's vomit was still on his sleeve and shoes. Reina had the mop bucket in place, gloves hanging from one finger.

"No," Eli said, reaching for the roll of red biohazard bags. "I'm not leaving this all on you."

They worked in silence for three minutes, bagging soaked paper towels and gloves while the exhaust fan buzzed over them. Eli scrubbed his forearms to the elbow in the sink until the skin went blotchy and raw, then stood there staring at the running water like he had forgotten what came next.

"I got the rest," Reina said quietly. "Go do your report before she starts."

She meant Kline.

Ms. Kline waited by the audit desk with a clipboard tucked against her chest, thumb flicking its edge in a fast metronome. One open tin of cinnamon mints sat by her elbow.

"Mr. Mercer," she said, using the formal tone she used when she wanted witnessable professionalism, "I need a written statement with exact times and actions. Corporate risk's going to ask for sequence before dawn."

Eli nodded and sat, because standing made his leg shake. He typed while she dictated prompts.

2:49 notified by housekeeping of unresponsive employee. 2:50 attempted contact. 2:51 forced entry. 2:52 called 911 via housekeeping radio. 2:53 naloxone dose one. 2:56 naloxone dose two. 2:59 EMS arrival. 3:08 transfer to stretcher.

He knew how this language worked. It converted panic into bullet points and stripped every sentence down to defendable verbs. It mattered. It kept people employed. It kept lawsuits contained. It also made him want to put his fist through the monitor.

Kline read over his shoulder. "Good. Add that security officer Darryl Pike assisted with airway management. Keep it factual, no conjecture regarding substances."

"I know," Eli said.

Her eyes flicked to him, registering tone. "I know that was rough. We still need your station reopened. The front desk line is already backed to the velvet ropes."

Eli's jaw locked. He tabbed into the wrong field twice, backspaced hard enough to tear a line in the printout, and heard the policy distance in every uncontracted word.

Eli looked past her to the camera feed wall. In one screen, slot machines blinked red and gold in absolute indifference. In another, two guests argued over a taxi. Life had resealed over the crack before the body was even out of the building.

"Give me ten minutes," he said.

"You have three."

"I need ten."

Kline pressed her lips together, then checked the staff board clipped near the printer. "You're still in probation status. Refusal to work assigned post can be coded as abandonment."

He almost laughed at the word. Abandonment had become the coin everyone carried for him, stamped with different logos depending on who was spending it.

"Code it however you want," he said. "I'm taking ten."

He stood before she could answer and walked through the service exit into the loading dock alley, where cold desert air hit the sweat on his shirt and turned it to film. The dumpsters hummed from compactors cycling two bays down. A truck idled at the dock, reverse alarm chirping in slow, patient beeps while a driver in a ski cap smoked and stared at nothing.

Eli sat on an overturned milk crate by the cinder-block wall and put his head in his hands. His palms smelled like latex, bleach, and somebody else's vomit. He rubbed them on his pant legs until the skin burned.

He had seen people in crisis before, professionally, in antiseptic offices and committee meetings where the stakes arrived as charts. He had reviewed wrong medication counts, unattended bed alarms, and residents found in stairwells. He had written corrective-action memos with verbs like mitigate and remediate. He had believed, on his best and worst days, that if he could keep sequence tight he could hold collapse at the edges.

Inside the restroom, sequence had mattered. It had also not been enough. Mateo might still die by sunrise. The body did not negotiate with checklists.

Another memory opened, uglier this time because he had spent years sanding it down. Theo at twenty-two, three months before rehab, locked in the upstairs bathroom at home while Nora pounded the door with the heel of her hand hard enough to bruise. Eli had been on speakerphone with his boss in the hallway, whispering that he might be ten minutes late, trying to keep his voice neutral because he hated sounding out of control. Theo had finally opened the door with pupils like pinholes and a nosebleed he kept wiping with toilet paper that disintegrated across his fingers. Eli had said, "Get cleaned up," like they were late to church.

He bent forward until his forehead touched his knees. Gravel pressed through the thin fabric at his ankles. He had told himself his job required composure, that somebody had to keep income steady, that panic did not fix addiction. All of that had been partly true and mostly convenient.

The burn mark above his eyebrow itched where scar tissue tightened in cold. He scratched at it and looked at his watch. 3:19 a.m.

His first urge was old and automatic: lock this down, get back upstairs, finish shift, send money, never mention tonight to anybody who could use it as proof against him. He could already hear the script forming in his own voice. He had handled it. Everybody was fine. He could get back to work.

He saw Mateo's bracelet again, cheap beads cutting half-moons into skin while Eli counted breaths.

Everybody fine was a lie that arrived before evidence.

The second urge came slower, which made him distrust it less. Call Theo before the shift swallowed the night and turned this into another private file in his head. Call while his hands still shook. Ask for structure he did not control.

He hated how much that sounded like surrender. He hated that surrender might be the only useful word left.

His flip phone sat in his locker beside the green folder. He went back in through the dock, ignored Kline's glance like a held blade, and walked to the staff room. The folder was bulging with crumpled wire receipts, debt notices, and the copy of Nora's voicemail transcript he had printed at the public library because hearing it once had not been enough punishment. The pages tracked mortgage arrears, the medical plan, and June's meal plan cut.

He pulled the phone, the folder, and Theo's old road atlas from the locker. The atlas was soft at the corners now, pages dog-eared and stained with coffee, Theo's handwriting in the margins where he had marked cheap motels and truck-stop showers. At the back, Eli had started listing transfers with dates and amounts in pen that skipped on damp paper. The list looked like a child's attempt at order: 180, 240, 90, 300. It had no cadence and no system, only guilt converted to whenever-he-could.

He went to the employee stairwell where the camera had a blind angle under the landing and called Theo.

It rang five times. On six, Theo answered with ambient warehouse noise behind him, forklift beep and an air compressor kicking on.

"Yeah."

Eli swallowed. "It's me."

"I know your number, Dad." Theo sounded awake in the blunt way people on early shifts sounded awake, no warm-up in the voice. "You okay?"

The right answer was no. Eli started with logistics anyway.

"I can only talk a few minutes. I'm on break. One of the night guys overdosed in the restroom. We got Narcan in him, EMS took him out, and now management wants everything normal in nine minutes."

Silence on Theo's end, not empty, measured.

"You hurt?" Theo asked.

"No."

"He alive when they rolled?"

"Yeah. Barely, but yeah."

Another pause. Eli could hear Theo moving, maybe stepping away from a loud bay door. When Theo came back, his tone had shifted into the clipped precision Eli remembered from the rehab years, the voice he used when he wanted facts to stop lying.

"Why are you calling me right now?"

There it was. Theo offered no sentiment tax and no small-talk detour.

Eli leaned against the cold rail. "Because I can't keep doing this as a private project."

"Say it cleaner."

"I need help managing money transfers. And I need somebody who'll call me on my bullshit the day I start spinning stories."

"Okay." Theo let that sit for half a second. "What happened tonight that made this matter now instead of last week?"

Eli closed his eyes. He could have dodged. He was tired enough to tell the truth by accident.

"He went gray on the tile," Eli said. "Same age you were when we started dragging you to intake and pretending we had this under control. I heard myself doing that old thing, numbers first, body second. I don't trust myself to run this alone anymore."

On the other end, Theo exhaled through his nose, a soft static burst.

"All right," Theo said. "I'll help. Terms first."

"Yeah. Give them to me straight."

"By Friday noon your time, I get a photo of your pay stub and every wire receipt. Not when you remember. Noon. If a transfer bounces, I hear it from you before Mom does."

"Friday noon. Stub and receipts. No surprises."

"Money targets stay fixed until I say otherwise. Friday, five hundred minimum to mortgage until arrears drops under two grand. Tuesday, two hundred to medical."

Eli pressed his thumb into the split in his knuckle. "Five hundred works if doubles hold."

"If doubles don't hold, you tell me before Friday morning. You don't spring a shortfall after the cutoff."

Theo kept going, voice dry as chalk. "I build a shared calendar tonight. Sunday and Wednesday, six p.m. Central, you pick up. Miss one, I call once. Miss two in a row, I step off and you call Mom yourself with the numbers."

"If I miss two, I don't get to hide behind you. I get it."

"No direct promises to June about money, school, or home dates. Facts only. What happened, what got sent, what's next."

Eli let that one burn for a beat. "I won't sell her a date I haven't paid for."

"And this part matters." Theo's voice flattened another notch. "You don't save me for blood-on-the-floor nights. You call on a boring Tuesday too, or this is theater."

Eli looked down at his hand, skin raw and red over the knuckles.

"I can do that if it's on a clock," he said. "If it's scheduled, I show."

"Good. Then first proof is tonight." Paper rustled near Theo's mouthpiece. "Cash-on-hand photo after shift. Exact bills. No rounding."

Eli pictured the little spiral anyway, cheap black cover and grease-dark edges, Theo's block letters marching down the page. Theo did not trust memory when a life could disappear in one bad afternoon.

The numbers made Eli flinch less than he expected. They were bigger than what he had been sending and smaller than what guilt wanted. They looked possible.

"I can hit that if I keep double shifts," Eli said.

"Then keep them until you can't, and tell me before you can't."

Forklift horns blared in Theo's background and a voice called his name.

"I got to move," Theo said. "I'm texting you an app link and backup paper version because your flip phone hates links. Take a photo of your current cash after shift and send it. No rounding, no estimates."

"Yeah. I'll send exact bills, no rounding."

Theo hesitated, then added, quieter, "And eat something before you sleep. Adrenaline lies to you."

The words hit his sternum like a palm shove.

"You too," Eli said.

"I'm already on oatmeal." A brief huff that might have been a laugh. "Cafeteria raisins. Pray for me."

The line clicked dead.

Eli stayed in the stairwell another minute listening to the building breathe through vents and pipes. He had expected humiliation and got it. He had also expected contempt and got procedure with care threaded through it so tight he almost missed it.

When he stepped back onto the floor, Kline pointed at the desk clock.

"You took twelve," she said. "The lobby's chewing through us."

"Then dock me twelve," Eli answered, and sat.

She stared long enough for him to hear the slots again, then set a revised task list beside his keyboard.

"Finish guest folios, then inventory discrepancy report. Don't discuss the medical event with guests."

"Understood."

He worked until dawn under lights that flattened everything to one color. At four-thirty he reconciled minibar charges from three rooms. At five he corrected a laundry variance where a cart had been logged twice. At five-forty he printed the incident addendum Darryl requested and walked it to security.

Darryl was rewrapping tape around his watchband at the desk.

"Hospital called," he said without preface. "He came up, then dropped again in triage. ICU took him. Could still swing bad."

Eli nodded once, because gratitude felt too large for his throat and fear had not moved an inch.

"Good work in there," Darryl said. "Most people freeze."

"I almost did."

"Almost ain't the report." Darryl tapped the paperwork with a thick finger. "Kline filed a probation addendum. Corporate risk wants a supplemental interview this afternoon."

Eli gave a dry half-laugh that sounded more like a cough and headed out.

Morning in Reno looked bleached, mountains cut sharp against a pale sky, snow only on the highest lines. He took the employee bus down South Virginia to the grocery counter with the MoneyGram window because wired money sent before sleep counted more than promises made after.

The grocery lights made every surface look the color of old milk. Eli stood at the counter with his check, his ID, and the green folder spread open like a legal argument.

"Two sends?" the clerk asked.

"Two."

"Fee on each line."

"I know."

He sent five hundred twenty to Nora's account with MEMO: MORTGAGE ARREARS - WEEK 1 STRUCTURED. He sent two hundred to High Valley Medical Center with his account number copied from the bill. Nineteen dollars in fees. Two stamped stubs slid under the glass.

He took photos of both with the flip phone, then another photo of the cash left in his wallet laid flat on the counter, exactly as Theo had asked: three twenties, one ten, two fives, four ones.

Theo replied in less than a minute.

`Receipts and cash count logged. Wednesday is the first boring-day call. Don't make me chase you.`

Eli leaned against the storefront window and closed his eyes. He was so tired his bones felt hollowed out. A man in paint-splattered boots stepped around him to buy a money order. Somebody in the parking lot played corridos through a blown car speaker. A delivery truck dropped metal ramps with a crash that echoed off the cinder block and made him flinch.

He imagined Mateo in an ICU bay under bright light, oxygen mask swapped for tubing, bracelet cut off for an IV, Sofia maybe still waiting at school for a pickup that might not happen. He imagined Kline forwarding his name to corporate risk with PROBATION in the subject line. He looked at the eighty-four dollars left in his hand and did the next transfer math twice, because one lost shift would wreck Friday.

On the bus back to the motel room he rented by the week, he opened the atlas to the blank inside cover and copied Theo's check-in dates in block letters so tired he printed like a child.

In his room, he set alarms for noon proof and the Wednesday call, drank a glass of warm tap water, and lay on top of the blanket without taking off his boots. The ceiling had a stain shaped like a coastline. The heater kicked on, coughed dust, and settled into a low continuous whir.

His body kept replaying the night in fragments: the stall door resisting his shoulder, Mateo's heel striking tile, Darryl saying again, Nia's hands moving fast at the mask seal. Between fragments, another image slipped in and stayed. He saw Theo at twenty-two, outside the rehab intake office in a plastic chair, eyes hard and scared, asking, "You staying for the family session or not?" Eli had said he had a meeting. He had gone to the meeting.

The phone buzzed once with a text from Kline.

`3:00 PM supplemental interview. Mandatory for probation file.`

He set one more alarm for 2:15 and put the phone on his chest.

On the nightstand, the green folder sat open to the two stamped MoneyGram stubs, clipped where he'd see them when he woke.

Sleep came in drops, then took him all at once. Nothing had been handled. Mateo could still die before lunch. One missed interview could cost him shifts. One missed check-in could end Theo's oversight and put him back inside his own lying voice.


# Chapter 16

The two stamped MoneyGram stubs were still clipped in the green folder open across his knees. At the first hard sway out of Reno, water jumped in the plastic cup on the fold-down table and traced a thin ring across the laminate. Eli caught it with his sleeve before it reached the paper. In the dark window he looked narrower than he had on the platform, turned sideways on the seat with his bad rib toward the glass while the roomette seemed to inch inward after the station lights fell away. The table kept trembling at every joint in the track.

He had labeled three envelopes in block letters before boarding: `CASH`, `RECEIPTS`, `DO NOT SAY THIS`. The last one already had five torn pages in it. He had started three different versions of the same sentence in his notebook and crossed all of them out hard enough to scar the paper. The train swayed left. His pen ran off the line. He wrote one more opening anyway.

Nora, this reads like I practiced it.

He tore that page out too.

If he started with how it sounded, he was already acting. If he started with why he left, he was already bargaining. He pressed his thumb into the old crack in the folder spine and looked at the receipts instead: two MoneyGram stubs from the grocery counter on South Virginia, one payroll cash-out slip from the casino office, and one handwritten list of dates Theo had made him keep. Each slip carried a date, amount, method, and confirmation number. Nothing lyrical fit there, and no room for mood remained.

A knock came against the sliding door. The car attendant, Randi, leaned in with the step stool tucked under one arm. She wore a denim vest over the railroad shirt and a brass pin shaped like a trout on the collar. Her left thumbnail was painted electric blue, the others plain, and she spoke with the calm patience of somebody who had explained the same five things for twenty years.

"You want me to make this down now, or you waiting till after Denver?"

"After Denver," Eli said. "If that's okay."

"It's your room. I'm the one with the blankets." She glanced at the folder and then back at him, clocking it without prying. "Dinner call's in twenty. Don't skip it. People skip and then buy stale chips at midnight and get mad at life."

He almost smiled. "Yes, ma'am."

"Don't ma'am me, you'll age me. Randi."

She slid the door shut with her hip and moved on, keys clicking softly at her belt.

He put the folder back in his duffel and tested the flip phone. The signal showed one bar, dropped to none, and then climbed back to one. It came and went with the cuts in the canyon wall. By the time the train bent along the Truckee River and the sky turned a bruised purple over the ridges, he had drafted and deleted six texts to Theo that all sounded like he wanted a medal for doing arithmetic.

In the dining car he sat across from a retired school superintendent from Sacramento who had brought her own tea bags in a sandwich bag and kept apologizing for the way the car rocked when she reached for salt. Her name was Lenore. She had thick white braids coiled at the base of her neck and a small notebook full of birds she'd seen from train windows over the years, each one dated and written in tiny block script.

"People tell me to fly and be done with it," she said, stirring tea with a wrapped straw. "Then I'd miss half my hawk notes and spill this tea on takeoff. Train's slower, but I can keep my notebook open."

Eli cut into chicken that tasted mostly of steam and pepper and said, "Maybe."

"You're heading home or away?"

The question was gentle and direct enough to hurt. He lifted his water glass and watched it shiver in his hand with the rhythm of the wheels.

"Home," he said. "This time for real."

Lenore nodded as if she'd heard the weight under the sentence and wasn't going to make him unpack it for a stranger over train chicken. She told him about a grandson in Boise who refused to wear coats because he said coats made him slower in kickball. She worried aloud that winter was going to teach him a lesson the hard way, then laughed at herself for worrying in advance. By the time dessert came she had listed three kinds of hawks she hoped to spot crossing the plains.

The ordinary talk steadied him for an hour. Then it left him worse.

Back in the roomette he climbed into the upper berth because the lower one made his hip cramp, and then immediately regretted it when his rib scraped the ladder edge. He lay on his back with the curtain half-open and listened to the rail seam beat beneath him: ta-tunk, ta-tunk, ta-tunk. Every impact moved through the thin mattress and into bone. Sleep came in shallow pieces and dropped out whenever the train slowed for a siding.

Near midnight, outside Salt Lake, the signal finally held long enough for a call. Theo picked up on the second ring.

"You all right?" Theo asked. He sounded winded, as if he'd just come up stairs.

"Yeah. On the train. I got bars for maybe three minutes if we're lucky."

"Skip the throat-clearing. Give me sequence: what cleared, what's still pending."

Eli sat up, knees knocking the berth wall, and read from the stub anyway, each number a rung he did not trust. "First transfer was one thousand four hundred at sixteen-oh-nine Nevada time, reference ending three-six-nine-one. Second was eight hundred sixty at sixteen-forty-two, ending two-two-seven-four. Plus four hundred from the payroll card cash-out. That one's pending till morning."

He heard himself sounding like a clerk with a head wound and kept going.

Theo was quiet long enough for Eli to hear the click of a lighter in the background, then the scrape of it closing without lighting. Theo had quit smoking three years ago and still carried the same red Bic in his pocket like a superstition.

"Okay," Theo said. "I can pick the first two tonight if the desk guy doesn't act like a cop. Third one I'll grab before shift. You keeping anything on you besides ticket money?"

"Two hundred cash. Maybe one-eighty by Chicago if meals run weird."

"Keep it. Don't get noble on me and show up broke."

"I'm not trying to be noble."

"Good. Because nobody asked for noble."

The train dipped; the call crackled.

Theo went on, voice tighter now, businesslike in the way he got when he was trying not to slide into old fights. "I'll pick it up tonight and move it to Mom. Before shift I'll grab the third and send proof. Don't narrate over any of it, Eli. Paper stands on its own."

"Yeah."

"And Eli?"

He had not called him Dad in months. Eli understood that was not punishment now. It was accuracy.

"Yeah," he said again.

"When you walk in that house, don't walk in with a speech. Walk in with what you did and what you paid back, then shut up long enough for people to decide what they can do with you."

The line hissed and then cleared.

"I know," Eli said.

"No, you know it in theory. I need you to know it in your mouth."

Eli closed his eyes. The curtain smelled faintly of bleach and old dust.

"I hear you."

"All right. Train dies on me, I'm not calling back till morning. Text when you can."

Theo hung up first.

Eli stayed in the berth and listened to the wheels until they blurred into a low metallic roar, like somebody dragging chains across concrete far away. He had spent weeks telling himself he would return with proof. The proof was coming together: timestamps, transfer IDs, balance remaining. The part that made his palms sweat was the other ledger, the one no app could print.

He climbed down, opened the folder again, and wrote a new page without crossing out anything.

I took $14,000 from emergency funds before I left.

I disappeared for ten weeks and let everybody at home carry the panic while I learned the sulfur-and-bleach smell of Glenwood nights.

I had an affair with Marisol. I know the burn mark on her right forearm because I put my mouth on it when she came off laundry close with starch dust on her shirt.

Theo has every transfer ID and can forward the full trail without me in the room. Nora gets to decide what June hears and when. I don't get a vote in that.

Then he stopped because the numbers still looked small next to the first line, and because writing Marisol in plain ink made his stomach turn harder than euphemism ever had. He capped the pen and pressed his forehead against the cool window until the vibration numbed his skin.

At dawn the train climbed into a stretch of snow-dusted cut banks and pale grass. He washed at the metal sink down the hall, bracing one hand against the wall when the car pitched. In the mirror his healing scar made a pink hook near his temple, and the bruise-shadow under his left eye still rose when he was tired. He looked like a man who had been in a fight he started and then lost halfway through.

Randi was in the corridor folding used towels into a blue cart with geometric precision, corners lined up like she was setting bricks.

"You sleep any?" she asked.

"Some," Eli said.

"That's the rail special. Ninety minutes if you're lucky. Coffee's fresh for another fifteen, then it tastes like burnt pennies."

He took the warning and followed the smell to the service alcove.

By midday, crossing into open country where the horizon flattened and refused to move no matter how long he stared, the phone held signal long enough for texts.

Theo: Picked up both. Third cleared. Money moved. Sending all now.

Then, a minute later:

Theo: Sent 2660 to Mom from my account. IDs 88417 and 88421. PDFs in her inbox and mine.

Another minute:

Theo: She replied "received." That's all she gave me.

Eli read the three messages twice, then copied the IDs by hand into the folder. He did not answer for a full ten minutes because every response he drafted sounded like either apology theater or a request for emotional labor Theo had not offered.

Finally he sent: Thank you. Keep me on calendar. I land tomorrow evening.

Theo wrote back: Already did.

There was no heart emoji, no good luck, and no father-son script. The clean edges of it made Eli trust it.

The harder call sat under all of that like a live wire.

He put it off through Nebraska and into Iowa, through a late lunch where he could not taste the tomato soup, through a station stop where people smoked in tight coats under a hard blue sky and checked their watches as if time might be bargained with. By sunset he had opened Marisol's contact eight times and closed it eight times.

In the motel office near Glenwood Springs she had kept a chipped yellow bowl on the desk for loose keys and aspirin packets, and she always turned the deadbolt with two sharp clicks before counting the cash drawer. Those were practical habits, and none of them were mystical. The first night he slept there he woke to sulfur from the springs and bleach from the linen room and heard her in the hall laughing once, low and rough, at some story from the grounds crew. The second month she stopped laughing that way around him.

When the train hit a long straight run after dark and the car settled into a steadier roll, he took the phone to the vestibule between cars, where the metal plates flexed under his boots and cold air leaked through the seam in a thin knife.

She answered on the fourth ring.

"Hey," she said. Her voice was flatter than usual, tired. He could hear a television in the background and the soft hollow thunk of an ice machine dumping cubes. "You still moving east?"

"Yeah. Somewhere in Iowa."

"I hate that state on principle. Too many billboards for God and fireworks."

He almost laughed and didn't. "You at the desk?"

"Laundry room. Night clerk called out. I'm doing both. What do you need, Eli?"

There it was, clean and unsentimental. No invitation to warm up. He gripped the metal rail. The cold went straight into his palm.

"I wanted to tell you the money went through Theo tonight. Two transfers and a payroll cash-out. He sent confirmation IDs to Nora."

"That's logistics. I asked what you need."

The train lurched. He widened his stance and felt his rib protest like a thumb driven into a bruise.

"I needed to tell you I wasn't dodging," he said. "About leaving. About us."

"You're still doing the long runway," Marisol said. She was quiet, which was how she got when she was done tolerating spin. "I'm standing by a hot press with bleach in my throat. Give me the line, not the setup."

He stared through the dark glass at his own reflection, a ghost in a cracked window.

"I'm not coming back," he said. "I'm ending this. I'm not asking you to wait, and I'm not going to call like we're half-together in different states."

In the pause he heard the squeal of a laundry cart wheel through her side of the line, then her breath moving once, deliberate.

"Okay," she said.

The word hit like a brake check. His knees knocked the metal threshold and his stomach dropped.

"Okay," he echoed, because he had nothing better.

"Don't echo me," she said. "Use your own words."

He swallowed against the metal taste in his mouth. "Okay. In Glenwood I told Nora I was sleeping in the storage room behind the office. I wasn't. I was in your bed most nights after desk close. I told you I'd stay two weeks, just until my rib settled, then I kept taking your room key and your towels for almost two months. When I left for Reno, you worked my last two laundry closes by yourself because I bailed before dawn and called it a clean exit. It wasn't clean. It was me handing you my mess."

On the line he heard her breathe in hard, then let it out through her nose like she was counting to keep her voice level.

"That's closer," she said. "Now it sounds like you were actually there."

He waited. The train roared over a bridge. Wind hammered the vestibule door.

"You want me to make you feel clean before you get there?" she asked.

"No."

"Good, because I won't."

He pressed his knuckles to the rail until his fingers cramped.

"Are you okay?" he asked, then hated himself for it as soon as the words left.

"No," she said. "I'm pissed and tired and my forearm's on fire because the press jammed again and I pulled a sheet wrong. But I'll be okay. Those are different answers."

She had always been precise that way, specific before comfort.

"You going to tell her about me?" Marisol asked.

"Yes."

"When?"

"Before I start pretending I'm fixed."

"That's not a date."

"When I get in."

"Good." Another pause, softer now. "I didn't ask for forever from you. I asked for plain. We got there late, but we got there."

A door banged somewhere behind her. She muffled the phone and cursed at somebody in Spanish, sharp and efficient, then came back.

"I gotta go reset a machine before it floods."

"Marisol-"

"No," she said, her voice flat and final. "Save the finale. The rinse cycle's screaming and you already gave me the part that mattered."

He closed his mouth.

"Take the sulfur and bleach out of your jacket before you walk into your house," she said. "Nora will clock it in one second."

Then she was gone.

He stayed in the vestibule until his fingertips went numb and the screw head in the rail stamped a crescent into his palm. The lies lined up in order while the screen dimmed: storage room, bad signal, just two weeks. The affair was not weather. It was a schedule he accepted, nights he chose, and a woman with an inflamed forearm finishing closes after he left. If Nora called that motel, Marisol would answer in plain nouns, and Eli would have to live with whatever Nora decided to tell June after that. He had chosen it. Now other people got to choose what came next.

Back in the roomette he lowered himself onto the seat, careful with the rib, and opened the notebook to a clean page.

He wrote the order and forced himself not to decorate it.

Transfer proof first: IDs 88417 and 88421, plus the $400 cash-out.

Then the blunt line: "I took $14,000."

Then the line that burned: "I had an affair with Marisol in Glenwood."

If June asks where I was, give dates and towns, not a father's speech.

Do not ask Theo to translate me to my own family.

If Nora says basement first, go to the basement first.

He read the list once and let it stay ugly.

Outside the window the dark plains carried scattered farm lights like dropped screws on black felt. The train horn blew long and low at crossings, a sound that seemed too big for the narrow track it came from.

He slept better after that, not deep, but in longer stretches. In one dream he was back in the casino laundry with lint stuck to sweat on his forearms, shoving hot sheets into a press that kept spitting them out folded wrong. Mateo stood at the far table laughing at something Eli could not hear. Then the laugh cut off and the machine alarm started, and he woke with his hand clenched around the blanket seam.

Morning outside Chicago came gray and damp, freight yards and brick backsides of warehouses streaked with old rain. Passengers lined up in the aisle with backpacks and neck pillows, everybody impatient in polite American ways. Randi moved through them with her key ring and her trout pin and said, "Backpacks off your shoulders, let the red cane through first, and nobody misses this stop," to no one in particular and everyone at once.

At Union Station he had forty minutes before the eastbound connection. He bought ibuprofen, two bananas, and a bottle of water that cost more than it should have. In the waiting area he sat beneath a mural of steel workers and opened his email at a kiosk terminal with a temporary code Theo had texted.

He had three new messages.

From Theo: PDF attached, transfer confirmations. Timestamped 9:14 p.m. and 9:22 p.m. CST.

From Nora: Received.

From Theo again: I put both confirmations in the shared folder too. Don't lose your paper copies.

He tapped print and got a red banner: SERVICE DESK PAYMENT ONLY.

Track 14 would board in twelve minutes. He could keep the PDFs on his phone and save the cash in his envelope, or he could buy hard copies now and board hungry.

He took the cash line. The clerk chewed mint gum and fed each page into a printer that squealed like a belt slipping. The first copy came out with a black bar over one transfer ID. Eli paid to run it again. By the time he jogged for Track 14 with two clean sheets warm in the folder, he had burned twenty dollars he had planned to keep for the last leg, and the ache in his side was sharp enough to make him breathe shallow.

On the second train, somewhere east of South Bend, he finally texted Nora with arrival information and no framing.

Train 43 due 6:40 p.m. tomorrow if on time. I have the paper trail in hand and every ID logged. I can wait at station or get a motel if that works better.

The typing dots appeared, disappeared, appeared again.

Nora: Come to the house after 8. June has class and shift. We'll talk when I'm back from work.

Then, after a pause:

Nora: Bring every document.

He typed yes and changed it to Okay.

The rest of Indiana and Ohio passed in rain bands that blurred the glass and turned field drainage ditches into strips of tin. He watched wipers on passing trucks beat frantic time and thought about how long he had spent trying to phrase damage into something smaller. Rain stitched and unstitched the window. He took his ticket stub from his pocket and wrote in the margin with the dull train pen: arrive, answer what is asked, send Friday's transfer.

In the dining car that evening a kid in a Notre Dame hoodie dropped a fork and swore softly; his mother laughed and handed him a napkin, and the kid pretended he was too old to need help. Eli looked away because the moment was ordinary and unbearable in equal measure.

After lights-out he lay in the lower berth this time and let the ache in his side spread where it wanted. He did not rehearse. He followed the route in his head instead: old steel towns, river bends, the tunnel before Pittsburgh where the windows turned to mirrors and every passenger had to look at themselves for thirty seconds whether they wanted to or not.

Late the next afternoon the train slid along the Monongahela under a low white sky. He saw warehouses, graffitied retaining walls, church spires, and the familiar ragged edges of home. His phone buzzed once.

Theo: You in yet?

Eli: Ten minutes out.

Theo: Remember what I said.

Eli: I do.

His phone buzzed again with Nora's name.

Nora: wait outside until I open the door. Basement first. No June's room tonight.

Eli: Understood.

He zipped the green folder into his duffel, checked for the receipts one more time, then stopped himself from checking again. On the platform at Pittsburgh the cold was wetter than out west, a river cold that climbed through shoes. People surged toward pickups and ride shares, toward hugs, toward nobody.

Eli stood still long enough to let the crowd pass around him. The train door folded shut behind his back with a pneumatic sigh. He adjusted the duffel strap off his sore shoulder and walked toward the station exit without calling anyone to come get him.


# Chapter 17

Outside Pittsburgh station he read Nora's text three times before he crossed to the bus stop: wait outside until I open the door. Basement first. No June's room tonight. By the time he turned onto his block, the sky had gone the color of old dishwater, and the porches up the street had their blue-white holiday lights on timers, blinking into life one railing at a time. He stopped on the third flagstone of the front walk and waited exactly as instructed. His duffel strap cut into the ridge where his cracked rib had healed wrong, and the ache rode with each breath. The rust stain still spread under the hose spigot; the mailbox post still leaned where he had promised to reset it three summers in a row and never did. Nothing looked dramatic. That was part of what made it harder to stand there.

Nora opened the front door before he touched the bell. She wore scrub pants under a heavy cardigan and her hospital badge was still clipped to the hem as if she had forgotten it was there. Her wrist brace, the beige one she used on lift-heavy weeks, showed at her cuff. She did not step forward for a hug and she did not step back to make him feel unwelcome. She moved aside exactly enough for him to enter.

"The basement door is open," she said. "Bag can go down first. Then come up to the table."

He shifted the duffel strap off his sore shoulder. "Okay."

Radiator dust and lemon cleanser hit him first. Under that sat the crueler layer of home: the pine cleaner June hated because it made the kitchen smell like a dentist office, the wool throw on the couch, the onions Nora had chopped before she left for shift this morning and forgot to cook. Eli set his duffel on the basement stairs and touched the railing, where one spindle had a strip of black tape from a failed repair. He had put that tape there the week before June's sixteenth birthday. It was still there.

In the basement, Nora had cleared space beside the old workbench. A narrow bed sat against the cinder-block wall with a folded comforter and two towels stacked at the foot. His winter coat hung on a screw hook. A plastic drawer tower held clean socks, plain T-shirts, underwear, and one set of thermal layers. No decoration, no speech. Silent mercy.

He came back up and found the dining table laid out in stations. At one end sat his green document folder, swollen now with transfer receipts and pay stubs from Reno. At the other sat a legal-size envelope with Nora's handwriting: `SEPARATION TERMS - READ FULL`. Between them lay a yellow pad with boxes drawn in ruler-straight pencil lines, dates down the left edge. Nora had already filled the first three rows.

She pointed at the chair across from her. "Sit."

He sat. The chair legs made that scraping noise he had always meant to pad with felt. The sound bounced in his teeth.

Nora slid the yellow pad toward him. "I'm going to do this once, cleanly. If you need clarification, ask. I'm not doing emotional sidebars while we cover terms."

He kept his hands flat on the table. "All right."

"This is legal separation, not a rehearsal separation. Papers are ready. We sign tonight. Filing goes in Monday morning." She tapped the first row. "You live in basement space only. You can use kitchen, basement bath, and upstairs hall bath for shower. No entry to my bedroom. No entry to June's room. No knocking on June's door unless there is blood or fire."
